A SNAKE STORY.


MALAYSIA
FOR THE BULLETIN
HER FATHER'S HEADS A BORNEAH
NIGHTMARE.
“ Pasa Antu 1” “A spirit hath passed !”
and M ilita’s agate beads rattled to her trembling,
as something swift and silent brushed past her
little brass-burdened ear. Again it came, a
wavering shadow in the moonlight, and now
Malita laughed : it was only a big brown goat-
sucker, after all !
The laugh was low, a mere ripple on the smooth
of midnight silence, but Tambat, awake and star-
gazing, heard it. Neither poet nor philosopher
was Tambat; he was an older institution than
either, the quidnunc; and, peeping from the
window in his nipa-thatch, he saw the dark form
gliding from hut-shadow to hut-shadow.
A woman ! —young, alone, and making stealthily
for the pangarang. Tambat’s portentous mouth
expanded in a grin that halved his pock-pitted
f ice. Here was the making of a pretty scandal !
In the pangarang—the “ head-house ” —slept the
bachelors of Surah ; in part to guard that sacred
citadel; in part to keep them out of amorous mis-
chief. And here was mischief going to them ! —
slim young mischief in bedang and bracelets, with
shining beads about its graceful neck, and long
hair streaming to its slender waist.
Out of his eager way exultant Tambat kicked
his slumbering wife, and soon across the moon-
strips a second shadow glided after the first. At
equal pace, with equal silence, went they, till close
before the girl—a black triangle on the moonlit
sky—the pangarang reared up its pointed roof.
* # #
Into the head-house loft, through many a
lifted roof-trap, the moonlight and the night-
breeze came together, and made between them a
grotesquer horror than ever pork-supped artist
dreamed of. Around the circle of the roof, in
close and curving rows, the grisly trophies hung ;
dim blots of greater darkness in the shadow, hut
starting out, in p itches where the white light fell,
into hideous caricature of humanity, with huge
blank orbits where were one time eyes, and shark-
mouth-; grinning to the moon in ghastly travesty
of laughter. Among them heads not yet too stale
to load the air with fulsome carrion smell; with
others, dry a id hollow, fantastically nodding to
the breeze that frisked where once the brains had
worked.
Into this monstrous death-scene came suddenly
a warm young life; Malita dropped lightly from
the roof and looked around with staring eyes of
fear. The sin ill was nothing to her jaunty little
Dyak nose ; the heads her long-lashed Dyak eye
regarded no more than so many cocoa-nuts. Col-
lectively, that is, for there was one particular
brain-case she had come to seek—her father’s,
lopped a month before to “open the mourning”
for a Surah chief. A score of miles, alone and
quaking, had she paddled up the Antu-haunted
Dubur, and it was the head-house Antu now she
dreaded, as thus, in the second morning hour, she
stood panting with the grinning dead above her
and the murderous enemies of her tribe asleep
below.
Not all asleep ! Boki’s even'ng meal of rotten
eggs and stinking blue-fish h d be in over-ample.
He floundered, snorted, and woke—awoke to hear
upon the floor above a sound too slight for any but
a savage ear. Again it came—a faint concussion
on the hard bamboo, and up rose Boki, spear in
hand, and noiseless as a cat. It might be an
Antu, he thought, or, worse still, a Buau ! But
then, again, it might be the Sibis, or the Batsas—
stolen in upon them to requite a hundred cruel
-slaughters of the past. Not as an old woman
was Boki going to be jeered at! For himself
would he see who was above—or what ! So Boki
laid down spear, took parang in teeth, and had
climbed a dozen notches of the ladder-post, when
—clump ! —came something on his ugly skull, and
down went Boki like a stone !
# # #
Malita, climbing like a monkey from cross-
piece to cross-piece, had found, ere long, her
father’s head. In one of the moonlit patches she
came on it—half baked, half putrid—but recognis-
able still, and breathless Malita, incautious in her
gladness, dropped hardly feather-like enough upon
the floor again with her prize. Into the darkest
comer, with the noiseless swiftness of the savage,
she sprang, and, cowering, listened. Had they
heard ? rvo sound came from below, but that
proved nothing, and intently from her corner she
watched the black ladder-opening the moon-
lit floor.
Ay, there it came ! —a head ! “ The body must
never follow,' thought Malita as up she sprang,
and, with a wild swing of the cord rove
through the paternal temples, brought
down the dead skull on the living one
with a crash that scared the flying-foxes
squeaking from the eaves and brought to
their feet in an instant the sharp-eared
slumberers below. Wondering much,
they poked and pushed the flaccid and
oblivious Bold, and, wondering more,
they heard descend upon the floor above
a pounding shower of heads. “ The
Antu ! ” whispered they, and, trembling,
turned their eyes away from the trap
down which a couple of the ghastly things
had rolled. But, then, the voice of Tarn-
bat, shrieking wildly from above, dis-
solved the spell.
“ Come up ! —come up hither, ye men
of Surah ! Ye are robbed ! —robbed, I
tell ye !—and by a woman !—sleepy-
headed sons of bats that ye are ! Come
up ! —come up !”
They went up, and Tambat told
them. How he had followed to the head-
house a woman of the Sibis, and how he
had climbed in at a roof - window after
her. How, missing his hold, he had
caught at, and broken, one of the head lines ;
and how, as he picked himself up from amongst
the fallen heads, he saw the woman vanish through
the roof again.
“ A head had she in her hand,” he said, in con-
clusion. “ Therefore, say I, ye are robbed, and
heavily will ye have to answer it to the Orang !”
Whereat the playful Tambat chuckled—but not
for long. Bold, with many a grunt and growl,
had picked up his wits again, and, no sooner had
he grasped the situation than, with a single word,
he brought the injudicious Tambat’s mirth to an
abrupt conclusion. “ Darkness !” was the word—
a Dyak equivalent, as Boki used it, “for “Dead
men tell no tales!” The rest agreed. What skull-
and-woman story was this, with which to insult
the intelligence of the bachelors of Surah ? And
if, as Tambat said, there was a head missing, his
own would just suit its place. It would be nicely
baked by the time of next official counting, and
no one would be the wiser !
Tambat implored—the head-watchers laughed ;
he cursed them—they knocked him down with a
kris-hilt; he shrieked—they gagged him with a
chawat, lugged him out into the jungle, and waited
for the sun to rise. With the first red rays they
took the chawat off, gave him a valedictory chew
of betel, calmly docked him of his head, buried it
for the time in a cane-patch, and pitched the car-
case to the ever-ready crocodiles.
* * *
Came round, in time, the ‘ ‘ counting of the
heads,” and Molat, the Orang-Kaya, found the
tally right. Quoth he
“Not for any other three would I lose this head
of Dipuk’s. He was a great man of the Sibis—on
whose tribe be curses ! Fresh is it, also, to a
wonder. Hardly seemeth it the head of more
than a moon and a-half !”
Which observation—seeing the head in question
had been exactly six weeks parted from the un-
lucky Tarabat’s shoulders —betokened upon Molat’s
part a thorough knowledge of the subject.
But Dipuk’s head, with Argus-feathers decked,
hung high in Malita’s house until she took a hus-
band. Me threw it to the pigs !
NO QUARTERI
Upon the low Javanese coast-village came
down the stifling darkness of a latitude that
knows no coolness —night Or day. The palm-
stems stood out black against the whiteness of the
surf, relieved, in turn, by a sable sea that hardly
answered to the swift blue gleams amongst
the piled-up fierceness in the west. Upon
a panting ocean and a gasping earth the
atmosphere lay dead.
Tamoka, watching the sky,
thought, “ Storm will there be,
and much darkness. That is
well!”
Dooshi, watching Tamoka,
thought, “ Thunder and rain
and great darkness. That is
ill! But I will follow !”
Anjam, blinking up between
them, said, “Much water;
good for the rice ! ” and fell
asleep over his cigar.
Anjam had four wives ; but
two of them didn’t count, be-
cause, like himself, they were
more antique than handsome.
The other two had, each, a fait
share of good looks and one child,
Tamoka’s, a sickly girl—Dooshi’s,’ a
sturdy male urchin, much valued of hit
father. With which father, neverthe-
less, the hatred between the mothers
had little concern —it was the
bitterer jealousy of prestige. Also,
it was the old story the one
had a secret of which the other
was in chase.
The first deep grumble came
across the sea. Dooshi woke
her lord, and, grumbling in his
turn, he waddled in-doors to his !
sleeping-mat. Dooshi, seated beside
him, kept one eye on her rival till
Tamoka, sliding suddenly down to
the beach, made off into the gloom,
Dooshi—of more substantial architecture—
had more difficulty with the ladder; but for a
friendly flash she would have lost the scent.
The broad, blue glimmer gave a glimpse of white
garments; Dooshi girded up her own and ran.
# # *
The raiu had come—and gone. The anti-iris
—the fabled upas-tree—dripped mournfully above
the door in the mouldering stockade, and Tamoka,
as she came forth, did not see the figure in its
shadow. But Dooshi stepped out —the lightning
showed her face —and for a minute the two fronted
each other in silence.
Said Dooshi, quietly, then —“ The place of
lepers ! ” Then, again, as the other made no
answer, “And thy child! —thy sickly brat!—it
will be thrust forth with thee, for this ! ”
“Thrust forth!” Tamoka echoed. “Why
should we be thrust forth ? There is no harm.”
Dooshi laughed as she flung away the hand the
other had placed upon her shoulder. “So little
harm that Anjam will cast thee from him like a
mangy dog—thou and thy leprous whelp ! What
man of these decaying wretches is its father ? ”
“No man of them, thou wanton woman ! It is
my mother who is here. ” Tamoka flung herself
upon the sodden ground. “ Have mercy, Dooshi,
thou hadst thyself a mother! ”
The lightning showed no mercy in the other
woman’s face. “ A mother had I,’ 5 she answered,
slowly ; “ but not hither would I have come to
see her. Igo ! Wilt thou come also—that I may
see Anjam spit upon thee for a leper and bid them
chase thee back hither with spears and curses ?’“
Tamoka sprang up and back. “ She-panther !
Hast thou no heart in thy body ? Wouldst thou
even come hither for thy child’s sake ? ”
“No !” Dooshi answered, and, with the word,
Tamoka leaped upon her. The two went down
amongst the soaking undergrowth, and there, like
tiger-cats, they fought in the lightning-broken
darkness till Tamoka, finding herself the weaker,
wrenched herself away and fled.
* * *
Dooshi had missed the track. The sun
was up when, with her tale, she came to Anjam.
He stopped her.
“The boy is gone,” he said. “Stolen whilst
thou wast away. Find him, thou; and that
speedily, or I will make of thee an example to all
wandering wives !”
Dooshi, rushing to and fro like a bereaved hen,
had a sudden inspiration that sent her back to her
angry spouse.
“Tamoka !” she had only time to say before
the old man, upsetting his morning rice, came at
her with his kris.
“ Find me the lad !” he called after her, as she
scurried down the ladder, “ then jabber of what
thou wilt!”
Dooshi took once more the track to the leper
village. “ Where that woman is,” she muttered,
“my child is.” But, presently, by the wayside,
she found Tamoka, waiting and alone.
“My child !” Dooshi cried, grasping at the
other’s arm, “My boy, Hagai, where is he?”
And Tamoka, pointing backwards, said, “Thy
boy, woman of no mercy, is where thou didst say
thou wouldst not follow him. I left him in a
leper-woman’s bosom !”
Without another word Dooshi was off—striking
against trees and tumbling over creepers in her
frantic haste—until, bleeding and panting, she
stood and smote fiercely upon the door in the
stockade. A man opened it; a creature so woe-
begone and dreadful that she started back ; but
then, remembering her errand, she rushed past
him into the enclosure.
Upon a miserable mango-tree in the centre a
couple of tame monkeys grinned and chattered,
and, clinging head-downwards to the bark, a
hideous hydrosaurus lizard sunned itself—unsoared
among a people too intimate with death to kill,
ahe air was foul with stench from stagnant pool
and rotten refuse, and a rank, green slime was on
the crazy huts from which, here and there, the
doomed looked out and wondered at this woman
rushing to and fro with breathless questionings
about her child. No children there, they said,
save two or three who had been born into horror.
But, if she would venture, she might search the
huts.
She ventured—as Tamoka had known she
would—until, in one dark hovel, a ghastly relic of
a woman, coming unawares upon her, fell upon
her neck and kissed her thrice.
“Greeting to thee, Dooshi ! ” croaked the crea-
ture. “ Tamoka’s mother am I!—ho !ho ! ” At
which Dooshi, shuddering, fled through the outer
gate and away.
And then, again, she met Tamoka—triumph in
her eyes, and the boy in her arms.
“In the jungle I had him hidden!” she said.
‘ Amongst the lepers he hath not been. But thou
hast—aha ! And my mother kissed thee, did she,
as I told her. And whom will Anjam spit upon,
now ? ”
The maddened Dooshi dashed at her, and, heed-
less of the screaming boy, the two rolled over and
over. But not for long. Tamoka, when she took
the boy, had also taken a kris
“‘ No!' Dooshi answered, and, with the word ,
Tamoka leaped upon her,”
It was her turn to show no quarter!
Then, with the lad, she went to Anjam and told
him all.
lie half-drew his kris then sheathed it again.
“Thy mother is thy mother!” he said. “iwill
not slay thee ; but take now thy girl and get thee
to thine own people. Well is it written —‘Women
are the gifts of the Devil!’ ”
SWAMP-SWALLOWED.
Across the long perspective of the man-
grove flats the sun glared fierce and crimson.
From the black mud-banks white, pestilential
vapors rose upon the torpid air. A subtler miasm
quivered over the long stretches of mephitic ooze,
against the sickly green of which gleamed out the
hideous reds and yellows of fantastic crabs that
scuttled- -as the boat came near—into pools pris-
matic with stagnation. Beneath the slimy surface
the banded water-snakes glanced to and fro :
from the reed-beds came in hungry swarms the
tiger-mosquitoes; and from the mangrove-roots the
sword-flies darted out in swift detachments upon
the half-nude rowers—sweltering in a bath of
vegetable fetor.
Of the white men one would have fired at the
log-like immobility of a crocodile basking un-
troubled of that “ instinctive terror of man ”
which is but one of man’s own anthropocentric
delusions. But the other said, “No !” He was a
tan-faced old fellow with a wide, grim mouth and
stiff, white, ape-like whiskers.
“ Fire a shot here and we may as well go back !
Curious acoustics, these places have. The report
of a gun wanders up and down the swamps and
creeks as if it was never going to stop !”
“ You’ve been here before, then?”
“Wouldn’t be here now if I hadn’t, and if I
didn’t count myself fever-proof. But as for you,
Green . ”
Green—young and smooth-faced, but big, brick-
red, and square-jawed—cut in, “ No more of me,
old man! I’ll see this thing through ! And,
anyhow, if I'm not fever-proof, I’m pretty well
past praying for by this time ! See ?”
“I see you’re good stuff, at any rate! And
nothing else will do for this job. Let’s sum up—
leaving out such trifles as heat and insects. Fever,
snakes, sunstroke, poisoned arrows from the
Dyaks, treachery from these chaps of our own,
and, after all— -perhaps nothing at the end of it!”
“I’m game for all but the last. Heavens 1
Redfern, that would be a crusher. ”
“ Crusher?” Redfern leaned over to the young
fellow. “This is it. I’m five-and-sixty; here’s
my first chance of a pile, and I’ve staked on it
whatever’s left me, say 10 or 15 years. It’s worth
it. But, you—you might have lived for 70 years,
yet.”
“ Confound it, man, you talk as if it was all up
with me. My chance is as good as yours, at all
events. ”
The other man said nothing. He had private
reasons for being of a different opinion.
* * *
Through pallid, steam - like mist—the
visible breath of Death—the rising moon made
faint light for the two men groping in the stinking
mud, and shrinking, now and then, from the
horrid, unnamed things their hands encountered.
For half-an-hour they toiled, till to the knees and
elbows they were foul with evil-smelling filth.
Then Green gave up.
( ‘ ‘ This is the place, right enough, ” he said—-
“ according to your map. T tangle between three
creeks ; gravel bluff at one end ; three mangroves
in the middle. And between them we’ve searched
nearly every inch. If your friend Kussoab wasn’t
a first-class liar, he was mad ! Either way, it’s—
ugh !” Something was wriggling from the mud
between his feet, and, springing smartly from the
spot, he slipped and fell backwards.
“ It’s only a goocha !” Redfern kicked the fish-
like saurian ten feet away. “ What a fool you
are ! Hullo ! —are you hurt ?”
For Green lay where he had fallen—not hurt,
but thinking hard. One hand, as he came down,
had touched a stone, and any stone, in that abso-
lutely rockless region, must be the stone they
sought—the stone which covered the treasure he
had till now but half-believed in. And with belief
came greed and quick resolve. A part!—he would
have all! “This old man’s day is past!— mine’s to
come ! And what I mean for him, no doubt, is
no more than he meant for me !”
All this in the ten seconds before Redfern
helped him to his feet. “ Let’s get back to the
boat,” Green said, then, “for the present, at any
rate. I feel infernally queer. ”
Back to the boat they picked their squelching
way, the elder man puzzled, but not yet sus-
picious. It was when they had drunk of the
“ gunny - water ” made of rum and quinine
that a glimmer of the truth came to Redfern, the
more readily that he had himself determined, if
the find were made, to “ lose ” his partner in this
hellish wet wilderness of Manduka. For thus
together do the wits of scoundrels jump.
* * *
Thicker still the ghastly fever-fog hung
round the shadowy boat, but the moon was in
mid-heaven and the light had increased,
when Green sat up and looked around
the boat—silent, but for the snoring of
the wearied Malay rowers. Neither was
there visible the inevitable cheroot-
glow which would have told of Redfern’s
wakefulness. Green stole from under the awning
and over the side.
Redfern, a minute later, raised his head and
listened —then took his rifle and slipped also away
into the half-illumined mist, which immediately
thereafter yielded up a dusky figure that got
hastily into the boat and woke its fellows, for the
Malay crew had, since dark, been one man short
—a secret watch upon the doings of the whites.
Then, by the light of a little cocoa-nut lamp, six
swarthy faces gloated over a broken kris —the
fragment of the blade rust-eaten to a rugged
skewer, but the hilt, of damp-defying gold, set
thick with stones that sparkled even through the
clinging slime.
# # #
The frogs forbore their raucous chorus-
alarmed by Redfern’s strident laughter.
“ What a guileless innocent you take me for !
You sneak back here when you think I’m asleep,
and you show me a hole that ” —with a sneer
“ somebody else made ! Really, now, young man
—which is to say, young scoundrel ! young thief !
you might credit me with some brains'. ”
Green stamped up a shower of mu I. “ Scoundrel
yourself ! Brains or no brains !—there’s the hole,
and I didn’t make it. And, besides, if I found
anything, where is it ? ”
Redfern threw up his rifle. “ Exactly !” he
said. “ That’s what I want to know. Where is
it?”
Green stooped his head. “Listen!” he said,
“there’s your answer !”
A hurried sound of oars came faintly through
the sluggish air, and both men made off, stumbling
and squattering in their haste to reach the main
creek.
They were too late ! A scornful yell from far
away in the fog was all that answered to their
shouts.


With the earliest grey of morning Redfern
sprang from the mangrove-root on which he had sat
silent for hours. “Come!” he said, “let’s get
away from here, at all events. Getting out of the
Manduka’s another matter !”
Green slowly raised himself from the mud and
lit his pipe. “ What chance is there of that ?” he
asked sullenly.
“ About one in a hundred !” Redfern answered,
belying his belief, for he was a man of
science, not ill-versed in jungle-craft, and he
thought the odds were in favour of his own escape
—unhampered by a tyro’s company.
He eyed his double-rifle as he headed westward
through the dreary, dripping mangroves. His
cartridges—save the two in the barrels—had gone
with the boat. “ I could not spare a bullet, even
if ” The'other called him, and he turned.
Green, up to his ankles in
mud, was dragging out each foot
in turn —only to sink the other
deeper as his weight came on it.
And the up-turned stuff—though
the surface was a glossy green—
was of a dirty saffron hue. A
“ porridge-pot,” Redfern knew it for,
and, on the instant,
turned and made off.
This—without his in-
tervention would
rid him of his in-
cumbrance, though
to see the poor
wretch slowly
perish there was

and, flinging himself full length upon the firmer
ground, thrust out the barrels to the utmost.
“ What did you say about the kris ?” he panted,
as he lay.
“ I have it!” Green gasped, as he got one hand
on the muzzles. “If I sink, it sinks with me !”
And, lying thus for life, he plucked the rifle
towards him—and the piece, exploding, sent its
bullets through his neck. Then Redfern, stretch-
ing eagerly to reach Green’s inside pocket—was
caught himself.
For twenty horrible minutes he fought, and
prayed, and swore, and yelled to earless solitudes.
And, when the noise had ceased, a monstrous
green batrachian —squatting on the double grave—
croaked solemnly that all was well!
“TO OBLIGE VANDORKENI”
Physiognomically, Yandorken was unfor-
tunate. Nature had provided him with the type
of head and face which is commonly, and, in the
main, oorreotly, associated with the more pro-
miscuous sort of amativeness. But, of the many
exceptions, Yandorken was one. His thick, red
nape meant nothing more than a large, good-
humored stupidity; the affection in his loose,
moist eye was but an affection for the kirschwasser
which, in long-continued doses, had relaxed his jowl
and thickened out his lower lip from beneath the
grizzled-ginger moustache. But his brother-
planters—knowing that in his freedom from the
kindred weakness he stood alone amongst them —
had, with humorous accord, conferred upon him
the title and the reputation of Don Juan. In
which way it came about that one night, at the
tail of a symposium chez Yandorken, the ebriously
facetious Bronker, getting unsteadily to horse,
hiccuped forth with incautious loudness ;
“ Good-night, you gay old dog ! Good-night,
my bold Don Juan ! —and beware the fate of your
namesake! ”
Yandorken shook his fist after the departing
Bronker. “ Idiot! ”he muttered. “If my wife
hasn’t heard you I’m in luck.”
He wasn’t. “And who,” quoth Vrow Vandor-
ken, suddenly, “was he? —Don Juan, I mean.
And why did that tipsy ass call you by his name ?
And what was his fate ? ”
Which trinity of questions so confounded the
unready Yandorken that, with match in one hand
and his final cigar in the other, he merely stood
and stared at her with his mouth open.
The lady, lean, acidulous, and older than her
mate, closed hers with a snap. “ Never mind !”
she said, “ It’s of no consequence !” With which
she went to bed, and Yandorken, smoking medi-
tatively amongst the fireflies, cursed exceedingly
the injudicious Bronker, and knew that there was
trouble ahead.
* * *
In the Netherlands, in Africa, in Guiana,
in the Indies—wherever you find the Hollander,
you find the most hospitable of men. A casual
introduction to Yandorken, in Bantam, and a
trifling incidental conversation, made me as wel-
come to him as the sea-breeze in the morn-
ing. None the less so, possibly, that the relations
between his vrow and himself were, even to the
unaccustomed eye, so palpably “ strained ” that
the stranger within them gates made early resolve
to get outside them again as soon as decency
would let him. And hugely was that stranger—
myself—astonished at the question sprung upon
him by his hostess, almost before the solemn,
white-robed mandoer had whisked the cover off
the soup. _ , ,
“Did you ever hear, Mynheer Englander, of
anybody called Don Juan ?”
Mynheer Englander, not without boggling,
replied that he certainly had heard of such a per-
son ; and, thereafter, being further interrogated,
euphemistically explained that the gentleman in
question had been credited with unconventionally
easy notions of morality.
But I wasn’t to escape that way !
“As regards,”said this uncompromising female,
“ as regards the other sex, I presume ?”
“ Hang it all !”• I thought, “ if the old girl’s so
plain, I don’t see why I should shirk it ! Yes,” I
said, “ the legend makes him out one of the most
unconscionable rakes and rascals that ever walked
the earth.”
And then—by the lady’s spite-laden glance at her
husband, as by the husband’s purple cheeks and
swollen eyeballs;—l saw that I had “ done it
though how, I hadn’t the remotest idea, till in the
creeper-clad verandah I sat and smoked with
Yandorken at midnight.
“ It wasn’t your fault,” my host said ruefully ;
“ how could you know? But the fact is that ever
since she got hold of that infernal nickname she’s
been three times as jealous as before. And that,”
added Yandorken, with doleful reminiscence, “is
saying a good deal!”
“But, till to-night, she didn’t understand the
allusion.”
“ She suspected, my friend. And, now that she’s
sure, I won’t even be able to look at a woman on
the plantation 1”
“Perhaps—between men, you know !—perhaps
you have been looking at some of them a little too
“ The piece, exploding, sent its bullets through
his neck."
freely already ? Some of these Javanese girls are
very pretty.”
My host’s bamboo chair creaked under the
energy of his disclaimer.
“ Tuyfel, man ! —not I ! I wouldn’t care if there
wasn’t a woman on the face of the earth. The
fellows —devil take ’em ! —only gave me the name
on the what-d’ye-call-it—oh ! the lucus a non prin-
ciple. All the heart I’ve got’s in my tobacco —
growing, stripping, curing, and turning out the
best brand on this side of Jojorka. And smoking !
As for the wenches who work at it —some of ’em
pretty enough, as you say —I don’t believe I know
one from the other. ”
“All which,” I told myself, as I drew my mos-
quito-net for the night, “ sounds uncommonly like
protesting too much.”
* * *
It was a pleasant place, the drying-house
—with its grateful shadows darkened into indigo
by sharp, intersecting shafts of blinding tropic
sunlight; its subtly pungent atmosphere, laden
with nicotian aroma and the chatter of busy,
bright-clad girls—some slinging the green tobacco
to poles across the shed, some piling the yellowed
bundles upon bamboo frames, some pressing and
packing the matured brown leaf into mats and
boxes. Good-looking girls, many of them, in the
somewhat squab-featured Malaysian style, and
one, at least, an unquestionable beauty, even
according to European canons.
“She?” Yandorken answered to my question.
“Oh, that’s Halmaheira!” And Halmaheira,
looking up with a smile, showed us a wide white
mouthful of ivory, unstained of the accursed betel.
“Ha, friend Yandorken !” I said, “here’s one
you know from the others, at all events !”
The honest fellow blushed, as far
as brick-red would let him. “ Well,
she—she’s a kind of fore-woman,
you see. That’s prime stuff for my
own use she’s doing up in plantain-
leaf. Just go over and have a look
at it.”
I went over, but for once that I
looked at the weed, I looked a dozen
times at the fair manipulatrix there-
of ; and she, with laughter in her
long black eyes, made good her title
to that intermediate status between
coquetry and impudence for which
tire mysterious term “minx” ap-
pears to have been invented.
“ You’re a pretty girl, Hal-
maheira, with a pretty name,” I
said to myself, “ but you’re a
minx, for all that! And, perhaps,
after all, Mrs. Yandorken ”
A sudden widening of Halma-
heira’s velvet eyes made me look
round. J ust within the shed stood
Mrs. Yandorken herself, with a
white cotton umbrella accentuating
the leathery sourness of her face.
Yandorken’s surprise had stiffened
him into a ludicrous suggestion of a
soldier at “ ’tention five hundred
busy fingers had forgotten their
function, and a hundred sloe-black
eyes spoke blank astonishment; but
only of myself did Mrs. Yandorken
take the slightest notice—walking
straight up to where I stood
sheepishly flapping my cheek with
a leaf from Halmaheira’s table.
“ I congratulate you on your
taste, mynheer!”—indicating the
girl with a lean, yellow fore-finger,
“It is good; but unfortunately
you are not alone in it. lam sure
you would not willingly commit a
breach of hospitality, so it is but
fair to warn you that you are at this moment
poaching on your host’s preserves - !” Bho waved a
hand towards her husband and stalked solemnly
away.
Yandorken looked stupidly after her; I looked
at Halmaheira; Halmaheira laughed aloud, and
round the other girls went the gentle ripple of a
smile. Down to the little 10-year-old fly-flapper,
there wasn’t one of them that hadn't grasped the
situation.
* # »
“ You’ll have to get rid of the fair Halma-
heira,” I advised. “No peace till you do !”
Yandorken gazed out over the reed-beds for a
minute before he answered, disconsolately ;
“ Exactly ; but I’m not sure that she’d go !”
I looked hard at him. Was there anything
sub-rosd, after all ?
“No, no !” he answered to my inquiring eye.
“ Not that! It’s this way ! This Halmaheira’s a
mischievous little animal. She’d stop at nothing
to spite my wife —or myself, either, after I’d sent
her about her business. She’d keep hanging
round, and turning up at awkward times—making
things ten times worse for me, in fact!”
Yandorken mopped his face and groaned—then,
with a s iddeu brightening-up—
“ Hemell—l have it I You’re going back to
Bantam, aren’t you ?”
“ Yes ; what’s that to do with it ?”
“Everything, my friend ; you’ll take her with
you !”
I laughed at him. “Declined with thanks,” I
said.
Yandorken chucked away his cigar among the
crocodiles, and nearly tumbled me into the tank
after it, so eagerly did he clutch me by the arm.
“ You don’t understand ! Here’s what I mean!
Halmaheira will go with you readily enough ; the
notion of a white husband would fetch any girl in
Java.”
“Daresay ; but ”
“Wait! Of course you’re not going to marry
her —needn’t even speak to her on the journey !
You’ll be on horseback, and I’ll send her in a
tandoe. And I’ll give you a cheque on De Rioter’s
for 500 guilders, which you can hand over to her.”
“Very tidy solatium, too! Well, Yandorken,
it’s a middling steep business ; but you’re a good
sort —so, if you think the money’ll do instead of
the man ”
“She’ll jump at it, sir! No disparagement to
you, but she’ll simply jump at the cash.”
# * *
With Yandorken’s parting words in my
ears, I was easy enough in mind till I dismounted
at the door of Portegra’s and was struck dumb
by the appearance of my charge as she emerged
from her tandoe. I had only hi herto seen her in
working trim, for it had been dark when we left,
and at the rest-houses I had carefully avoided her;
but, now, here she was in a gold-embroidered
kabaya of blue Bugis silk, with a silver skewer
through her ebony top-knot, broad gold stripes in
her muslin sarong, and big silver butterflies on her
little red slippers. It was simply gorgeous, and
her eyes were truly very fine and dangerously
reproachful., Yet I tried to harden my heart, got her
into the hostelry, and made straight for De Rinter’s
—whence, with the 500 guilders (about .£4O) at
which my damsel was to “jump,” I sauntered
contentedly back—glad to be done with an un-
pleasant job.
» * *
But Halmaheira didn’t “jump” —didn’t
show the remotest inclination to skip. In the best
Javanese at my command I explained, and ex-
plained again ; and still, without a word, she kept
gazing mournfully from the money to the man and
from the man to the money. And still I talked,
and felt every moment more and more mixed and
mean and idiotic, until I went mentally to pieces
as the lady, with a soft smile spreading like the
dawn across her puzzled features, pushed back the
coin-representing rags, and calmly observed that
it was proper for the husband to have charge of
the money.
“The husband !” In something not unlike
despair I looked around. It was the native
section of the hotel, and, from the other
end of the room a portly old Javanese gentle-
man was considering us, not unsuspiciously,
through gold-rimmed glasses; while, nearer at
hand, two women of the upper-class—more richly,
if less garishly attired than my incubus—were
regarding her askance with all the fine super-
ciliousness proper to that circumstance. For which
reason Halmaheira—having, woman-like, observed
them without looking—determined, I suppose, to
take the wind out of their sails by showing them
what the person they despised might, could, and
would do with one of the august white race. Up
she jumped, at all events, and, before I altogether
realised that I had been energetically folded to
her blue-silk bosom, I was being kissed upon
either cheek with a rapidity and resonance that
drove the astounded Javanese ladies to the other
end of the room, where one of them, tumbling
over the petrified old gentleman, set up a screech
that brought waiters on the scene —then old
Portegra himself, with a couple of grinning Dutch
“I was being osculated on either cheek."
planters from the European side of the house.
“ Verdoemnis ! ” said one—“the Englander’s
doing well. I shouldn’t mind being in his place !”
I knew him ! It was old Jan Rittaker —whose
guest I had been—whose wife and daughters had
made much of me ! My cup was full, and I fled.
* * ' ■*
“ Oh, no need-a you go ! ” Portegra said,
when, in the mellow dusk, 1 had stolen back to his
place for my traps. “Fine girl dat, eh?” with a
wink of his rascally Portuguese eye. “ Peety
lose run of her. But she gone! She call you
plentee fool —pleutee veelain —but she go avay at
last! ”
Fool and villain ! Well, to this hour I don’t
know exactly which of these parts I played—to
oblige Yandorken !
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
The Rajah’s
[FOR THE BULLKTIN.]
In my garden, O Beloved 1
Many pleasant trees are growing,
Peach and apricot and apple,
Myrtle, lilac and laburnum.
Fair are they, but midst them lonely,
Like an exiled Eastern Princess
In a strange land far from kindred.
Stands a lonely fair Pomegranate,
Dreaming of its native Orient,
Always is the fair Pomegranate,
And beneath it I lie dreaming
Of thine eyes and thee, Beloved 1
Overhead its red globes gleaming
Like red moons, old tales recall of
Eastern moons and songs of Hafiz —
Nightingales and wine and roses.
And, at whiles, it seems a mystic
Tree Oircean whose red fruit is
Broken hearts of old-time lovers,
Their sad secrets once more telling.
And within each red sun-cloven
Glossy globe, like unto little
Hearts within a great heart glowing,
Glow translucent seeds of crimson.
Like the fruit of the Pomegranate
Pull of little hearts my heart is,
And the little hearts so glowing
They are thoughts of thee, Beloved 1
Hapily these at times are woven
In with dreams of the Pomegranate :
Thus, perchance, I dreamt the curious
Dream within a dream here written.
• . » • • •
In his palace-hall, methought, I
Saw a splendid Indian Rajah ;
Fame and Fortune were his vassals,
But his heart was sad within him.
’Round him stood his chiefs and captains.
“Great art thou,” they cried, “ O Rajah >
And thy hand is strong in battle.”
But he smiled not at their speeches.
Silently through his Zenana
Passed he, glanced with cold and careless
Eyes at women, fair as houris
Seen in visions bred of hasheesh.
Like to dawn, and noon, and starry
Night—like all the moods of passion—
Were they—rose-and-white Circassians,
Amber Hindoos, dark-eyed Persians,
Dancing girls with golden armlets,
Golden rings around their ankles—
Making music clear, melodious
As the plash of crystal fountains
Heard in still, hot nights of summer—
Danced the Lovers’ Dance before him.
But he heeded not their dancing.
For his heart was sad within him.
Thence unto his treasure-chamber
Strode he—there to gaze on gems that
Rajahs dead had won and hoarded ;
Tragic-storied, splendid jewels—
Diamonds, like fallen stars, for
Whose bright evil beauty blood in
Old days had been spilt, enough to
Make them burn like crimson rubies ;
Emeralds greener than Spring’s garments,
Pearls like unto tears of Peris
Weeping by the gates of Eden ;
Opals with their fateful lustre.
Long on these, and countless other
Many-coloured gems, the Rajah
Gazed, but found no more delight in
Their sun-flashing brilliant beauty.
He had dreamt a dream enchanting
Of twin-sapphires, blue as Heaven,
And his heart was filled with hunger
And with yearning to possess them.
Therefore unto his Vizier he
Told his dream, and gave command that
He should seek, the wide world over.
Till he found the wondrous sapphires.
Doth that sad Vizier still wander
O’er the earth the sapphires seeking ?
Sooth I know not —but I know that
He will never find them, never?
For they were no cold, bright sapphires
That the Rajah in his dream saw . . .
Waking from my dream I knew that
They were thy blue eyes. Beloved,
VICTOR J. JELKY.


THE J ADO CING OF
JACCER
FOR THE BULLETIN
When the 30th Dragoon Guards lay in
Ahmednagar, East India, private dohn dagger
was the smartest soldier in the smartest regiment
if the British army.
On these points there could be no doubt, because
Major Muller (commanding the 30th during the
ibsence on leave in England of Lieut.-Col.
“Jacky” Tulwar) affirmed them daily, and many
times a day, to the fatigue of officers and men.
In every corps is one man held by his com-
manding officer to be the sealed pattern of all
military excellences. These, in the cavalry and
in time of peace, include a small head, broad
shoulders and chest, flat back, slim waist, long
legs, and a swagger as of lord of all the earth.
Such virtues dagger had, adding thereto six feet
three inches of height, straw-colored hair, bulging
blue eyes, a big moustache, a pink complexion and
countenance. His enemies—envy ever
dogs perfection—swore he could not ride, and
likened him to a “sack of sand tied up ugly.”
But the Major, reared in a foot regiment, had
for the unsightly equitation of his favorite the
fellow-feeling which makes wondrous kind.
* * *
Before “ Jacky,” as officers and men called
him, went on leave—he had only been gone four
months —Shoeing-Smith Ned Fee was esteemed
the pattern man of the 30th. He was a most
running worker in iron. All that might be done
with a hunk of metal and a horse’s foot he did.
He rode superbly, and anything. ‘ ‘ That sahib talks
the bat of the horse and the horses talk to him, ”
said the troop yhorawalas. “The dead spit of a
dragoon,” was the verdict of those rigid critics, his
comrades. Slight, yet powerful, five-feet-ten in his
hoots, trimmest of soldiers, and, for a white man,
a wonderful swordsman, Shoeing-Smith Fee was
from top to toe the ideal cavalryman. His skin
was nearly as dark, and his features quite as clear-
cut, as the skin and features of a high-class
Brahmin. The blood in his veins was gypsy blood.
He loved and hated as gypsies love and hate. And
“ Jacky ” counted him as the pearl of price among
farriers and troopers.
But the Major, like greater autocrats when they
come to rule, broke, of malice and lust of power,
such of his predecessor’s idols and laws as. might
with safety be broken. He abased Fee and exalted
dagger. The latter marked his rise by an osten-
tatious bumptiousness which would have ensured
a smaller man many and painful thumpings. Ned
took his downfall, as he took all things, calmly.
Yet in many ways were rubbed into him his
inferiority and the superiority of the new favorite.
Both were in “A ” Troop. When that troop
came under the Major’s inspecting eye he failed
not to publicly berate Fee and belaud dagger. It
would be: “Your puggaree, sir, looks like a
damned debased sausage. Fold it as dagger folds
his !” Or: “ Why the devil don’t you wear your
forage-cap properly? Look at Private dagger !
He’s a pattern to the regiment. You look like a
half-baked militiaman, by God —you, Fee !” After
one of these diatribes the mutinous spirits in “ A ”
Troop would look sympathetically at Fee and
murmur, “ Oh, damn Private dagger !” But Ned
said nothing and looked nothing.
There were other trials of his temper. A great
assault-at-arms was announced to be held at
Poonah. Every regiment in the Presidency would
compete. The 30th were to send a man from each
troop. The barrack-rooms unanimously decided
that Fee, as the very best man in the corps, must
go. He did not go. The Major selected dagger
in his place. The latter took two prizes for minor
events. But the pride of place the 30th con-
fideptly counted on, had Fee been a competitor,
fell* to a much-scorned Hussar regiment. Yet the
Major, attended perforce by the band and volun-
tarily by his courtiers, went out long miles to meet
the returning detachment, dagger rode into can-
tonments on the Major’s charger, the superior
officer striding alongside, holding the private’s
stirrup-leather, while the band played “See the
Conquering Hero,” and the bandmaster thrust his
tongue into his lean cheek, and was inaudibly
blasphemous.
# * •
Shortly afterwards, one of England’s cruel,
twopenny-halfpenny wars was imminent. The
Major, by sweat ol Jaw, wrung a promise
from the Commander-in-chief that, m case
of hostilities, a wing of the 30th should
participate. Whereon the regiment went
crazed with blood-thirst, or the desire of
change at any price. What troops were to form
the service-wing ? what men were to belong to that
wing ?—these were the questions unceasingly and
hotly debated in bungalows, the sergeants’-mess,
and the canteen. They were soon set at rest.
“A ” troop, among others, would not go. Selected
men would be drafted from non-service to service
troops. Private dagger was among the elect. S.
S. Fee was not.
Th© night the final arrangements were published
in regimental orders, the 30th went on a wild
whooping tear, preparatory to sobering up for the
campaign. When the men of “A ” troop —all dis-
orderly, some half and others frankly drunk —
returned to their bungalow from bazars and the
canteen, dagger, mostly fairly temperate, partly
because his head was soft, chiefly because his dis-
“ Neither spoke. Private Jagger's eyes were very curious
position was saving, was pot-valiant. His normal
pinkness had merged into boiled-lobster tint. As
asual, the blare of his own trumpet was blustrous.
Fee sat quietly on his cot and smoked reflectively.
Then “ Skew ” Evans —smallest and most mis-
chievous man in the troop, Fee’s chum and a hater
of dagger, whose cuticle was never proof against
Skew’s mosquito stings—explained sweetly, to no
one in particular, but for public benefit :
“Service-troops, indeed ! Service-troops be
rammed, jammed, and double-damned ! They’ve
been made up of all the hugliest and hawkwardest
men—the regimental helephants with glanders and
farcy, wot the major wants killed horf before the
regiment goes ’Ome.”
“ Blind me ! Do you mean that for me?” bel-
lowed dagger.
“ Take it any bloomin’ way yer likes, Gerliarth,”
said Skew, with the languid hauteur of a little and
impudent man.
Jagger plunged up the bungalow, proposing to
“ knock that there Skew’s infernal ’ead hoff.”
But, when he let out viciously at the small man,
who dodged scathless, Ned Fee put down his pipe
and stood up before the bully:
“ You hit me —hit a full-sized man, Jagger, for
a change like 1 ”
Jagger paled, scowled, and, speechless, made as
if to stalk away, while Fee, smiling, resumed his
seat and pipe.
Hardly had he turned his back when Jagger,
wheeling, struck him a crashing blow behind the ear
which knocked him senseless athwart his cot.
Skew, howling with rage, tore his carbine from
■thth, butt k desperately at JaggerB head

Had aim and intent been equal this story might
have closed here, and Private Jagger might have
been saved some future trouble ihe carbine fell
on his shoulder like a horse s kick and took all fight
out of him. It was good for Jagger that yust
then the troop sergeant-major, rather ful of drink
and very full of discipline, came in and ordered

Skew quietly brought his chum to. There 11

that great hulking beastly coward, Jagger,” was

Next morning, after Skew had breakfasted,
cleaned his saddle and burnished his accoutre-
ments, he loafed down to the forge where Fee was
fitting a shoe to a horse s foot and not the foot to
the shoe. When are we gom to thump that
swme Jagger, eh, Ned • _ _
Fee went calmly on with his work. “ There’ll
fight just now,” he said.
“ Wh-a-a-t ? ”
“It’s beer-time ; go down to the canteen and
get drunk, you little beggar, and don’t come
growling here. There’s plenty of time for dagger’s
business—better ways than fighting. Wait a bit !”
This was a long speech for Fee.
Skew cut up some plug tobacco slowly and
scientifically, rubbed it between his palms medi-
tatively, filled his pipe with agile thumb and fore-
finger, and lit it with a red-hot iron. He knew
something of his chum’s disposition. A light
broke upon him, and he went away whistling
softly. “ Ned’s up to some of his gypsy hanky-
panky. God help Jagger !” he muttered, and bore
up for his midday beer with placid mien and
healthy thirst.
# # *
Perhaps Httie Skew al °ne, and he dimly,
comprehended why Fee suddenly went on the
spree, or, at least, commenced to spend money
freely at the canteen—which was not his custom.
Not even the sagacious Skew divined why, one
night soon after the quarrel, Ned, a big pot of
rum slightly diluted with hot coffee in hand,
walked up to Jagger and invited him to “drink
hearty.”
To this invitation there were, considering the
relations of the two men, but two possible answers
—refusal and prompt war, or acceptance.
dagger’s conceit obviated the former. He under-
stood the proffered pot to be a natural and proper
tribute to his worth. He drank affably and
heartily. Thenceforward a friendship appeared
to exist between the late rivals. Presently they
went on shooting excursions to the canteen and
bazars together, while all their little world
wondered, and Skew was green with the bile of
jealousy.
Jagger and Fee left barracks one afternoon in
company. The former had taken to drinking a
good deal, in a circumspect way, at Fee’s expense.
He was feeling the effects. Ned admitted a “sore
head.” He knew where some liquor might be had
“on the sly.” They-Struck across the dry and
dusty maidan by a path unknown to Jagger, and
came out in a tope of trees in which were two small
huts—one at either end. Said Fee, “In one of
these is a native who sells brandy. I don’t know
in which one. Go to the further hut and see if
the black pig is there. I’ll look in here !”
* * *
There was a sensation in “A ” troop at
roll-call that night—private dagger was “ absent
without leave.” There was a pattern man,
indeed ! There was the Major’s pet ! And the
Major had sworn that any man guilty of the
slightest offence should not go on active service !
He had sworn it by several gods and many devils.
Long after “Lights out” “A” troop discussed
this astounding event excitedly, but without
sympathy for dagger. Skew leaned over from his
cot and whispered to Ned :
“What ’ave you done with the swine—knocked
’im on’s soft, squashy ’ead, and buried him ?”
“What do I know?” said Ned, carelessly.
“ He left me in the bazar."
dust before orderly-room next morning dagger
turned up. nuit.e Br>Vw»r, but dazed looking. “i on
might ’ave ’ung your forage-cap on his bloomin*
bulgin’ eyes,” remarked Skew.
dagger was marched in front of the Major, and
his crime proved by the troop Orderly-Sergeant,
whom the Major promptly and virulently attacked
for some alleged irregularity in dress. “By God,
sir,” he thundered, “I’ll reduce every lout of a
non-commissioned officer in the regiment. I’ll rig
the triangles, and flog the regiment from right to
left. I’ll- .” Then he cooled suddenly, looked
at the prisoner ? and said, “Dismissed ! ” dagger
saluted, went right face, and swaggered over to his
bungalow, more offensively blatant than ever.
Fee asked him, “Where did you get to yester-
day?” “Nowhere in particular,” replied the
other, frigidly. And this, it was observed, was
the last conversation in which these two ever
indulged.
That night, dagger was again absent. He re-
turned thirty-six hours later, more vacuous-look-
ing, if possible, than before. “ Reprimanded ”
was, to the profound astonishment of the regiment,
the Major’s brief sentence. At night, he was
again missing. This time, he stayed away three
days. Now, such a crime would have insured any
other man cells, at the very least; possibly a regi-
mental court-martial. The Major awarded “ three
days confined to barracks,” and even the subser-
vient little Adjutant nearly choked with indigna-
tion. Certainly the Major’s language to the
prisoner was lurid and scathing. But what is
wrath which evaporates in profanity ?
That same night dagger broke arrest and was
absent. He did not come back for four days.
This time the Major did not storm. He spoke
to the prisoner seriously and quietly, and in his
gentlemanly manner—as was the Major’s way
when really dangerous. He said : “It is evident
to me you do not go away to get drunk. You are
either in the toils of some vile native harlot, or you
are a coward and want to avoid active service.
You will not succeed in that. Whatever you do
you shall go, if I have to tie you to the tail of a
bullock-cart and drag you. Now, what is the
matter with you ?”
The big creature, ordinarily so braggart, burst
out blubbering : “I can’t help it, Major. Wish I
may die, sir, if I want to get out of goin’ on ser-
vice. For God’s sake, Major, sir, lock me up till
the order to march conies. Court-martial me, sir
—anythink !”
“No,” said his commanding officer, reflectively ;
“no, I’ll give you one more chance. I’ll trust to
—to your honor as a soldier. Return to your duty
and behave like a man.”
-Tagger attempted some reply, but sharp and
short came the regimental Sergeant-Major’s—
“Salute! right face! quick march!” The man
slunk over to his troop. Afterwards Major,
Adjutant, and Provost-Sergeant—the latter a gray,
grim, and wily old non-com.—held long conference.
* * *
At midnight, when “A” Troop snored
with unanimity, dagger got slowly up from his
cot, sat awhile on the edge cogitative, and began
to dress. Presently, as if thinking better of it, he
lay down. Ten minutes later he sprang up, his
eyes fixed., his mouth working. Once more he
seemed to conquer an almost irresistible impulse
and fell back with a groan. Half an hour passed.
Then he dressed quickly and resolutely and went
out of the door, through which the moonbeams
gleamed, as if dragged by a rope. Fee at that
moment n.iied himself on his elbow, stared at the
retreating figure, and laughed softly and unplea-
santly. Some two hours after, dagger, handcuffed,
was brought into cantonments by the Provost anti
lodged in the guardroom. Next morning Provost-
Sergeant Clink reported to the Adjutant :
“According to orders, I watched ‘A’ bunga-
low last night with Privates Ball and Curtis. At
12.30 Private dagger came out, dressed, and headed
across the maidan by a disused path to north of
the fort. Followed him upwards of three miles.
Then entered a tape, which I have not seen before.
There were two huts—one at north end, one at
south. He went to the north

* * *
“ Here, read these reports,” said the
Adjutant, who was sweating at his quarterly
returns, early next morning, to the doctor of the
30th, who rode up to the orderly-room for five
minutes’ chat before breakfast.
The doctor, three-parts of whose life had been
passed in India, read. Then he puckered his
mouth into queer shape.
“Did you ever hear of such damned folly?” said
the Adjutant.
“Rawlinson,” remarked the doctor, “you are
an awfully decent little fellow. You know more
about the pipeclaying of a pouch-belt, and —if you
are only given time—can get the regiment into
better line than almost any man of your inches.
But you know rather less, man, of natives and
what they can do than you know of —of the way
to Heaven!”
“ Well, then, how do you account for it ?”
“ I don’t account for it. The woman’s influence
over Jagger may be hypnotism—a word we use
to conceal our sickening ignorance. But how a
woman disappears at will—well —l give it up. ”
“Gh ! thanks, very much,” said little Rawlin-
son.
# * *
What the Major said, when Jagger was
brought in front of him, was, “ Seven days’ cells !”
What the Adjutant said when, next morning,
the Provost-Sergeant reported that Jagger had
broken out of cells—though the lock was ap-
parently untampered with and the sentries had
seen no one pass—and that he (Jagger, or the
remains of him) had been found dead, prone on
his belly, before the hut at the north end of the
type, was, “Well, I’m damned !”
J. EVISON.
Maoriland,
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Maoriland, my mother I
Holds the earth so fair another ?
Oh , my land of the moa and Maori,
larlanded grand with your forests of kauri,
Lone you stand, virgin beauty your dowry,
Maoriland, my mother I
Older poets sing their frozen
England in her mists enshrouded ;
Newer lands my Muse has chosen,
’Neath fair southern skies unclouded ;
Set, a solitary gem,
In Pacific’s diadem.
Laud of rugged, white-clad ranges
Standing calm, impassive, lonely ;
Ice and snow, where never change is
Save the mighty motion only,
Where through valleys seared and deep
Slow the serpent glaciers creep.
Land of silent lakes that nestle
Deep as night, girt round with forest—
Water never cut by vessel,
In whose mirror evermore rest
Green-wrapt mountain-side and peak
Reddened by the sunset’s streak.
Land of forests richly sweeping,
By the rata’s red fire spangled ;
Where at noonday night is sleeping,
Where beneath the creepers tangled
Come the tui’s liquid calls,
And the plash of waterfalls.
Land where fire from earth’s deep centre
Fights for breath in frenzy furied ;
Till she from the weight that pent her
Flings her flames out fiercely lurid ;
Where the geysers hiss and seethe
And earth shudders far beneath.
Land of tussocked plain extending
In the distant blue to mingle,
Where wide rivers sigh unending
Over weary wastes of shingle,
Cold and yellow is their flow
From the glacier-ice and snow.
Land where slow streams dream and dally
’Neath the toi’s floating feather,
Where the flax-blades in the valley
Whisper stealthily together,
And within the cabbage-trees
Hides the dying evening breeze.
Land where all winds whisper one word,
“ Death !” though skies are fair above her !
Newer nations white press onward,
Her brown warriors’ fight is over —
One by one they yield their place,
Peace-slain chieftains of her race !
Land where faces find no furrow,
With the flush of life elated ;
Where no grief is, save the sorrow
Of a pleasure that is sated.
Land of children lithe and slim,
Fresh of face and long of limb.
Land of fair, enwreathed cities,
Wide towns that the green bush merge in ;
Land whose history unwrit is—
Memory hath no chaster virgin !
Land that is a starting-place
For a newer, nobler race !
Maoriland, my mother !
}fylds the earth so fair another ?
Oh, my land of the moa and Maori,
Garlanded grand with your rata and kauri,
Lone you stand, virgin beauty your dowry,
Maoriland, my mother !
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
Dunedin, Maoriland.
THE BUNDARY RIDER'S STORY
FOR THE BULLETIN
The storm that had been brewing all the hot
afternoon, gathered, towards nightfall, in grim,
black clouds, cleft every now and then by jagged
streaks of vivid lightning. Just after dark it
burst in a fierce rush of rain and boom and rattle
of thunder. Blinding as the lightning was, it was
only by its assistance that a belated traveller
could keep his horse on the bridle-track he was
following ; for when the darkness fell between the
flashes it was as though a black pall h.'.d been
dropped over everything.
With heads bent down the sodden man and
horse plodded on until the rider found himself on
a main road into which the track debouched.
“ Another mile,” he muttered to himself, “ and
I’ll come to old Mao’s. ” He touched the horse
slightly with the spur and glanced nervously
round. The travelling now improved, and ere
long a dim light ahead proclaimed his approach to
some kind of habitation. Soon afterwards he
pulled up at the verandah of a small bush inn.
“Are you in, Mac?” he roared with a voice
which outdid the thunder, as he splashed down
from his horse into a pool of water and hastily
proceeded to ungirth.
“Who’s there?” returned a voice, and the
owner came out and peered into the darkness.
“Smithson ! Lend us a pair of hobbles.”
“ Jupiter ! what are you doing out here such a
night?” asked Mac as he handed the hobbles.
“ ’Cos I’m a fool, that’s why,” said Smithson, as
he stooped down and buckled the straps. “ Can’t
go wrong for feed ?”
“Right up to the back door. Come in and get
a change.”
Hanging his saddle and bridle on a peg driven
into the slab, the late traveller followed his host
into the bar. Mac put his head out of the back-
door, and roared to somebody to bring in some
tea ; then reached down a bottle and placed it,
with a glass, before his visitor. Smithson filled
out a stiff drink, tossed it off neat, and gave a
sigh of satisfaction. Having got a dry shirt and
trousers, the traveller proceeded to simultaneously
enjoy a good meal and his host’s curiosity.
“ It’s that cursed Chinaman hunted me. The
one who cut his throat.”
“Did you see him?” asked Mac in an awed
voice.
“ I did, indeed, with the bandages round his
neck just as they found him. I meant to go in to
the station and tell the boss he must send out
somebody else. Then I remembered that you
were nearer and came over for a bit of company.
Now, don’t laugh at a fellow—just you go and
stop in my hut for a night or two.”
“No fear,” returned Mac emphatically.
“ Well, I thought I didn’t care for anything,”
said the boundary-rider, “ but this caps all. You
should see —oripes! what’s that!” For a long, lugu-
brious howl sounded outside, followed by the rat-
tling of a chain. Both men forced a very artificial
laugh.
“It’s Boxer,” said Smithson, suddenly illumined.
“ I left him tied up, but he’s got his chain loose
and followed me. ”
A very wet and woe-begone dog came in at his
call. Smithson detached the chain from his collar,
and they sat down again.
“ Boxer didn’t fancy being left alone,’’remarked
Mac.
“ Seems not. It gave me quite a turn when he
howled like that. What do you say ?” Taking
the hint, Mac arose and the two went into the
bar.
“ All alone to-night ?” asked Smithson.
“Yes, the missus is in town ; there’s only deaf
Ben in the kitchen. ”
“ Well, I hope that d d Chinaman won’t
follow the, dog. ”
“Don’t get talking like that. How did he come
to out his throat ? ft was before my time. ”
“ The fellow who had the contract for the pad-
dock-fence lived in the hut with two men, and the
Chinaman was cooking for them ; he was there for
over six months. Chris., the contractor, he paid
off the other men; and he and the Chinaman
stopped for a week longer to finish up some odd
jobs. One morning Chris, came in to the station
as hard as he could split—the Chow had cut his
throat the night before. Chris, said he wasn’t
quite dead, and that he had tied up the wound as
well as he could. The super, and another man
went back with him, but when they arrived the
Chinkie was as dead as a door-nail. Now, the
strange thing was that the stuff Chris, had tied round
his throat was quite clean ; but when they moved
the body, with Chris, holding the shoulders, the
blood commenced to soak through, and turned
them all quite faint. All Chris, knew about it
was that when he awoke in the morning the
Chinaman was lying outside with a sheath-knife
in his hand and his throat cut. ”
“ And did you see nothing until to-night ?”
“ No. Just after dark I heard someone calling,
and I went to the door and looked out. I can tell
you I just did get a fright, for there by the
lightning I saw the Chinaman standing, with
bloody rags round his neck, and the knife in his
hand.”
Mac shuddered and passed the bottle.
“Now,” said Smithson, “comes the strange part
of it. That shout, or coo-ee, I heard, came from
some way off, and that there ghost I saw was not
looking at me, but listening for that shout, and
smiling like a man who was expecting a friend
coming.
“ What did you do ?”
“ I slammed the door to, picked up my saddle
and bridle and got out of the back window. I
knew my old moke was not far off, but I was that
soared I left his hobbles where I took them off. I
heard Boxer howling as I rode away.”
* * *
It was. a beautiful morning after the storm as
Smithson rode in to the head station. So bright
and cheerful was it that the boundary-rider felt
rathef ashamed of the yarn he was going to tell
and half-inclined to turn back. However, he went
on and had an interview with the superintendent.
Naturally he was laughed at, and this, of course,
made him stubborn.
“ I’ll tell you what I’ll do,” said Morrison, the
super., at last. “I’ll go back with you this
afternoon and stop the night with you, and we’ll
see if we can’t quiet the Chinaman. ”
Smithson agreed, remarking that perhaps it was
only on one particular night he walked, as he
had never seen him before.
Morrison turned up an old diary and glanced
through it. “ Oh, that’s nothing,” he said, push-
ing it away.
* * *
The two men rode up to the lonely hut, Morri-
son slightly ahead. “There’s something queer
there,” he said, pulling up. Smithson stared
eagerly; while Boxer, who was with them, sat
down on the road and howled dismally.
Recovering themselves, the two rode on. A man
was lying in front of the door stretched out in
death. Dismounting, they approached and ex-
amined him.
“ No, no !” cried Smithson, “don’t touch him
we mustn’t till the police come.”
With an impatient gesture Morrison stooped
down and turned the dead face up. In the throat
was a rude wound, and in the open eyes a terror
more than human.
“It’s Christy,” said the superintendent in a
quiet voice.
“The man who employed the Chinaman ?” asked
Smithson in an unsteady tone.
“Yes. How, in the name of God, did he come
here ? Tell me exactly what you saw and heard. ”
“ I was in the doorway, as I told you,” said the
boundary-rider excitedly, “standing just there,
and by the flash of lightning I saw the Chinaman
here ” —and he indicated the spot. “He was
standing like this ” —and he bent forward like a
man watching and listening—-“looking in the
direction the cry came from over there.”
“ And you saw a knife in his hand ?”
“Yes, and he was smiling as though expecting
somebody he wanted to see badly.”
Morrison put his hand on the other’s shoulder
and pointed to the knife in the hand of the corpse.
“ Was it that knife ?”
“ It looks like it,” chattered Smithson.
The superintendent glanced about and shook
his head. “No tracks to tell tales,” he said.
“No, all the storm was on afterwards.”
Morrison mused a bit. “ Get a sheet of bark,”
he said, “or one of those sheets of iron there.
We must put him inside, and then I’ll give you a
letter to take in to the police. You can get back
with the sergeant by to-morrow morning, and
we’ll bury him.”
“ You’re not going to stop here?’’said Smith-
son.
“ No,” returned Morrison. “I’ll get over to
Mac’s.
They lifted the dead man on to the sheet of iron
and carried him into his old dwelling - place,
Smithson evidently much averse to the job. Mor-
rison tried to close the staring eyes before they put
one of Smithßon’B blankets over the corpse ; but
the lids were rigid. “Evidently he didn’t like
the look of where he’s gone to,” he muttered. The
two set out—one to the little township, the other
to Mac’s pub. .
The night was as calm and fine as though ram aii 1 1
lightning and thunder were unknown. Morrison
mused deeply over the tragic occurrence, trying to
recall all he knew of the past and put a common-
sense construction on it, but he failed and only
made himself nervous.
“ There’s one very strange thing about the
affair,” he said to Mac, when they were discussing
it that evening. “When I paid Chris, for the
contract —over which he lost, by the way —I gave
him separate cheques to pay off his men, including
one for the Chinaman of about .£3O. That cheque
was not found, and, moreover, it has never been
presented to this day,”
“ What sort of feUow was Chris. ?”
“ A good fencer, but a stupid fellow, not fit to
take contracts. I often wonder if he took the
cheque off the body before he came in.”
“ Then why didn’t he present it ? ”
‘ ‘ That was his idea, no doubt, at first. But I
tell you he could scarcely read or write, and 1
suppose after he heard me tell the sergeant that
I would give the bank notice and instruct them to
watch for the cheque, he got frightened.”
“ Before that he imagined that one cheque was
the same as another, and that you could not trace
a particular one?”
“Yes, just about what he would think.”
“ Perhaps he killed the Chinaman ?”
‘ ‘ Perhaps he did, ” returned Morrison, after a
long and thoughtful pause.
By sunrise Morrison and Mao were at the
boundary-rider’s hut, and soon afterwards the
sergeant and Smithson arrived. The examination
did not take long, and they prepared to dig a
grave.
“ Better not bury him alongside the other,” said
the sergeant.
“ No,” replied Morrison. “Let’s see, we buried
him over at that tree ; didn’t we, sergeant ?”
“Yes, and not very deep either. It was dry
weather ; the ground was hard ; and we came upon
a big root. ”
The obsequies were not prolonged. Sewn up in
the blanket, the dead man was soon laid in a damp
grave. While the others were filling it up, Morri-
son, still thinking of Mac’s remark, strolled over
to the spot where the Chinaman slept, not expect-
ing to see any mark of the place left. He started
and turned pale.
“ Here ! quick ! Come here !” he cried. _
The men came hastily, the tools still in then-
hands. The earth over the old grave had been
loosened and disturbed.
“ My God ! he’s got up !” murmured Smithson.
“I’ve heard they can’t rest out of their own
country.”
“Give me a shovel,” cried Morrison ; and com-
menced to carefully scrape the earth away. The
sergeant assisted him, and they soon came to the
skeleton, for nearly everything but the bones was
gone.
“ What do you expect to find ?” asked the
sergeant.
Morrison was carefully brushing the loose dirt
off the thing with a bough.
“Look here !” he said.
Clasped in the fleshless hand was the missing
cheque.
“It wasn’t buried with him, I’ll swear,” said
the sergeant.
“And if it had been, it would have decayed
long since, ” answered Morrison.
“ He got up and took it from Chris, last night.
He was bound to get his cheque back,” said Mac.
“ Well, I’m going to pack up my traps,” re-
marked Smithson.
“I’ll send down and have this hut shifted,”
said Morrison. “Although, now he’s got what
he wanted, I don’t suppose he’ll get up again. ”
“ By gum, I won’t trust him,” said the boundary-
rider.
DELCOMYN.
There lives in a certain Australian town
a small and perky solicitor who had an ex-
perience once. Most solicitors have had an
experience once, but this one’s experience was
worse than the average. It was his duty,
in the interests of a client, to issue a writ
against a very large, freckled Irish person,
and the latter came in to ask for an explanation.
He was grieved, but calm, and carried the docu-
ment in his “ fisht.” In the other fisht he had a
stick. The small solicitor was alone ; he was sit.
ting at a table at the further end of the room, and
the Irish person was between him and the door.
When the visitor had scowled several times he
drove his hat firmly on his head, and the subse-
quent proceedings consisted of a monologue in a
powerful bog accent. “Sorr,” said the intruder
hitting the table persuasively with his cudgel,
“ye sint me this paper. Oi’ve just called to ask
what ye mane by it. If you can’t tell me what ye
mane by it, it’ll be the worse for yez. Spake now,
c. \’t yez ? ” The stick came down with a fearful
weit on the table. “If ye don’t spake Oi’ll
put yer skull through the window. Don’t preshume
to addriss me now. If ye dare to say a word to
me Oi’ll rub yer ear on the flure. ” The solicitor’s
knees smote together and he maintained a dead
silence, while his visitor hit the table in a fresh
place and resumed. “ Can’t ye spake ? Don’t
attimpt to bully me now ! Don’t use threatenin’
language to me or it’ll be the worse for yez ! Don’t
look at me with that overbearin’ expression ! If
ye think yer goin’ to frighten me ye’re very much
mi staken. Don’t attimpt usin’ bloodthirsty threats
to me, now, or O’ll brain ye with the chair. Oi just
kem in here to ask ye, calmly and quietly, what ye
mane by sendin’ that dirthy bit of paper to me,
an’ ye use violent language an’ trate me wid soilent
contimpt. O’ll begin to get angry soon. If ye
spake I’ll throw ye down the stair ! What did ye
do it for? What d’ye mane by it? Will ye-SPAKE?
Now don’t ye thretten me wid the law ! If ye dare
to say a wor-r-rd now —just one wor-r-rd
I’ll break the table wid yer head.” The
small solicitor sat with his mouth open and glared
at his visitor with a petrified expression, while
the Irish gentleman dented the table again with
his stick and grew purple with excitement, but
made a great effort to be calm. “Look here,
now,” he said, in a large, hot, moist whisper, right
into the miserable attorney’s ear, “Oi won’t
shtand much more of this. Oi kem here an’ asked
ye a civil question, an’ ye look at me with a mur-
derous expression, an’ ye won’t say anythin’, an
ye try to bully me. Are yez goin’ to explain Gl-
are ye not ? If ye say a wor-r-rd now Oi’ll flatten
yer head, so I will. If I hear ye flunkin’ in a
disrespectful tone Oi’ll tear yer ear off ! Oi’ll ”
but just here the diminutive lawyer made a
strategic movement for the door, and rushing past
the enemy did one long, comprehensive slide
down the bannisters, and arrived hatless in the
street. He has never served a writ on any but
the smallest kind of Irishman since.


BLACK
PETER'S
LAST
KISS
FOR THE
BULLETIN
It was the year 1840, and the hitter desola-
tion of a winter severe even for Tasmania lay
heavily upon all life at the Great Lake. Day
after day a watery sun glimmered fitfully along
the northern horizon, through storms of sleet and
snow that buried the few shepherds’ huts on the
deserted runs, and whitened the jagged “ Tiers ”
until they shone like icebergs. Lost in the clouds,
4000 feet above the sea, that lonely plateau seemed
given over to the powers of darkness; and Nature,
in all her aspects, grew loveless and terrible.
For, when the Polar tempest no longer lashed the
sullen surface of the lake, a deathly film of ice,
rapidly looping from headland to headland, blotted
out the bays and inlets, leaving only the far-off
surface of the central waters still gleaming,
trembling—and waiting.
To the solitary shepherd, doomed to winter in
those regions, what followed was even more awful
than that frozen silence. When the southern
winds swooped once again upon the prisoned lake,
great fissures and cracks tore and split shoreward
through the plains of ice with a sharp, incessant
crackling as of closest thunder. Daily thereafter
the angry waters drove the shattered ice upon the
northern coast, until Macarthur’s shepherd and
his wife, in their lonely hut by the shore, heard
once again the splash of the open waves, and the
crash of the piled-up hummocks grinding along
the shingly shore.
♦ * *
Macarthur’s shepherd was an elderly man,
like any other, but his wife was one in a thousand
—by the mercy of God. The chill twilight was
failing outside as they crouched together over a
roaring fire, and the woman cautiously unfolded
her plan.
“ Bill,” she said in her soft, drawling voice, “ I
found a hundred pounds yesterday.”
The old man looked at her curiously—they so
seldom spoke. “ Seein’s believin’, Bess.”
“ I tell you I’ve found a hundred sovereigns;
think of it, Bill—two years’ pay ! ”
A quick look of suspicion darkened his face, but
a moment later : “No ! no ! yer don’t find no
hundred pounds—leastways not in these parts
among poor young shepherds, and, anyhow, you
ain’t what you once was to look at.” He spat in
the fire and laughed coarsely.
She was strangely excited, for she allowed even
the insult to her appearance to pass unnoticed.
“ Bill,” she went on, half standing up as she
bent towards him, her forefinger trembling in his
face: “If you’ll only be a man to-night the
money’s yours—ours, I mean—we go whacks.”
“ Give over yer foolin’, girl, and say what you
mean. ”
“ Bill! Don’t you remember the notice on the
p’lice-station : ‘ A hundred pounds, dead or
alive?”’
The shepherd shrank from the strange force of
the woman as she stooped still closer and
whispered: “ I saw Black Peter on the marsh
yesterday—spoke to him—he’s coming to-night.”
The man started to his feet, his face white with
terror, and made a quick movement towards the
corner of the hut. She caught him by the arm :
“You know Black P« fr r's too cunning for that—
he’d have the first shoo
here like he’s always
had with the p’liee—
it’ll take the two of us
to do him.”
“My God ! And
lie knows I let on
about his camp in the
Cressy—Bess, we m ust
clear. ”
The woman let go
his arm and sat down
again on her box.
“Go if you like,” she
said slowly, looking
fixedly into the fire ;
“I’ll wait for Black
Peter: me and him
was old friends once
—he wouldn’t shoot
me. ”
She was playing him
like a fish. That senile
jealousy of his must be
made to take the place
of failing courage.

this morning to the

“What’s that to
you?” She was still looking dreamily into the
