ll©


HIS OTHER-
SELF
FOR THE BULLETIN
Dr. Margaret Lee was entertaining a few
of her women friends at afternoon tea. Two of
them had been chums of hers at Melbourne Uni-
versity, and had taken degrees in medicine in the
year when, as Margaret Mervyn, her brilliant
success at the examinations had placed its
proudest feather in the hat of Trinity Hostel.
She had married since then, but had kept up the
old college friendship—all the more easily, per-
haps, because she had not to count these two
among her professional rivals. They had never
been able to muster nerve enough to announce
that they were prepared, with drug and scalpel,
to practise upon the bodies of the Public; and,
having been bom with solid silver spoons in their
mouths, had, fortunately, not been driven to do
so by Necessity, which sometimes knows as little
of Medicine as of Law. Her other guests were
later acquaintances and admirers.
There was no suggestion of the New Woman in
the appearance or manner of Dr. Margaret. She
was distinctly, fascinatingly, feminine —a slender,
bright-eyed, red-lipped little lady—and yet ex-
tremely popular with members of her own sex.
This curious anomaly was probably accounted
for by her gift of humor —a quality rarely found
in a very pretty woman, and when so found, and
when it does not make them too uneasy, irresist-
ibl v attractive to other women because of its utter
incomprehensibility.
♦ * *
The conversation was beginning to flag
just a little —Dr. Margaret not being a person who
encouraged earnest discussion on the subject of
chiffons (if that is the right word) —when one of the
Bachelors of Medicine had a happy inspiration.
“Tell us,” she said, addressing her hostess,
“about the very strangest experience you have
had since you began to practise. The whole story,
please—if there is one.”
Dr. Margaret, smiling indulgently, commenced
—“ It was a Man.” Her audience became imme-
diately interested. “I need not describe him
further —it would be a breach of professional
etiquette to do so—than by saying that he was
tall, pale, and dreamy-eyed, and looked like one
who had lost something.”
“ His heart, perhaps,” murmured the other
Bachelor of Medicine, with a sympathetic sigh.
“ Nothing of the kind, Kitty! Something
much more serious, indeed. He began by telling
me that he had heard I was a Theosophist—which
was true enough at the time —and that it was
partly on this account, and partly because I was
young in the profession, and not likely to be, as
yet, a slave to old formulas and musty precedents
(his own words) that he had called to consult me
with regard to his own most singular case. Male
doctors, he said, and especially those of the old
established sort, could not be brought to believe
that anything transcending their own experience,
or the traditions of the profession, had any real
existence. They were, he said, mere gropers in
the dark when asked to deal with a case of a kind
not mentioned in their medical text-books. Of
course I stopped him before he said too much, but
1 must confess that I was not so greatly shocked
by his remarks as I might have been if my pro-
fessional brethren had treated me a little more
generously, when I first beganvto practise.”
Dr. Margaret laughed a tart little laugh, and
continued—“ I asked my strange patient to be
seated and to tell me what his trouble was. ‘ Dr.
Mervyn,’ he said (I was not married then), gazing
earnestly at me with his big black eyes, ‘ do not, I
implore, tell me that my liver is out of order and
that exercise and change of air, with the medicine
you will prescribe, are all I need to cure me. To
say so to a man in my hideous position would be a
piece of cruel irony of which I am sure one so
young and fair could not be capable. ’ The Man
was actually complimentiilgTne,but in such a cold-
blooded, obviously uninterested fashion that I had
no excuse for feeling either flattered or angry. But
I was certainly piqued. Previous men-patients
who had consulted me had been shy and diffident;
this one was perfectly cool and self-possessed.
* Sir, you have not yet told me the symptoms of
your complaint—if any,’ I said; somewhat sharply,
If am afraid. He did not appear to notice this,
however —in fact, he took very little notice of me
at all—but began his story—the queerest, most
extraordinary story I had ever heard. T had
1 tetter give it you in his own words, as nearly as I
can recall them.
“‘ I am,’ said
he, ‘ the victim of
the strangest mis-
fortune that ever
befell a human be-
ing. You see before
you the mere shell
of the man I was a
few short years ago.
The mainspring of
my life on earth is
broken. I am living
in two worlds at the
same time or,
rather, an emanation
from me is living in
the next world, and,’
h e concluded bit-
terly, ‘ carrying on
finely there at the
expense of what is
left of me in this
one. ’
“The Man spoke
coherently, and was
certainly sober.
Moreover, there was
the glitter of insanity
in his eyes, which
were soft and mourn-
ful in expression.
But his remarks
puzzled me. I sup-
pose he saw this, for
he smiled sadly and
observed: ‘You
think I am subject
to delusions ; pos-
sibly that I am a
morbid hypochon-
driac ! Pray don’t !
Nobody could take
more interest in life
than I did until I
found myself in my
present awful posi-
tion. I had—and
still have, when it
can no longer pur-
chase mo a single
pleasure a good
deal of money and
found plenty of en-
joyable ways of spending it. But I became
dissatisfied with the shortsighted day-to-day
life men live on earth, and began to regard
it as of no intrinsic worth, yet, to some ex-
tent, valuable as a preparatory education for the
next stage of existence. It was at this time that
my reading introduced me to the famous Theory
of the Law of Conservation of Energy in the Spiri-
tual World. You know that, according to this
theory, every deed done by a man in this present
stage of being becomes so much stored-up energy,
or capital, awaiting him when he is ready to com-
mence his career in the next stage of being. In
other words, a man at the moment of starting life
in the next world is the sum of his deeds in this
world, plus the motive power, soul, or principle of
continuity, which passes from his earthly body
with its final breath. You follow me, Dr.
Mervyn ?’
“I nodded affirmatively, and said it seemed to
me to be a very sensible theory. He said it was
more—it was a certainty, and one that involved
terrible possibilities. ‘ But what, according to
this doctrine, becomes of a man’s evil deeds ? ’ I
inquired.
“ ‘ They are set against his good ones, and if the
latter preponderate the balance of good is the
capital he has to start upon,’ was the reply.
“ * But if it is the other way about ? ’
“ ‘ Then he begins his new life in debt, to the
extent of the difference.’
“ I asked no more questions.
* * *
“ ‘ I lost no time,’ continued the Man, ‘in
making my conduct square with this new doctrine.
From that day forth my every action was done
with an eye to the increase of my bank-account in
the next stage of existence. I performed pro-
digies of virtue, taking infinite pains to evade
reward for them in this world. No slave of
Mammon could work harder in piling up gold
than I did in piling up good deeds to enable me
to begin my future life as a being of superior
order. And, even as his form of selfishness brings
its inevitable punishment to the slave of Mammon,
so did mine to me.
“ ‘I got into the habit of keeping a diary in
which I entered at night all the virtuous actions I
had performed during the day ; afterwards post-
ing them up in a ledger I had opened for the pur-
pose. I was thus engaged one evening when, all
at once, the feeling came over me that I was not
alone. I looked up and saw, hovering in the dusk
beyond the ring of lamplight, a faintly luminous
human shape, and heard a voice, saying, ‘Put
out the lamp ! ’ I did so, and beheld, shining
wondrously in the darkness, what looked like my
own reflection but highly etherealised just
stepped out of a full-length mirror. The shape
must have observed that I looked nervous, for it
said, with a smile, ‘ Don’t be frightened ! lam
only your other self.’
“ ‘You seem surprised,’ It continued, floating
into an armchair, ‘ yet the thing is very simple
when explained. Bach of your virtuous actions
implied a certain expenditure of vital force. Well,
when a given amount of this vital force had been
accumulated I was evolved—in accordance with
the universal law that life produces life —and I
think you ought to be proud of mb.’ I was, too,
although 1 could see, even then, Dr. Mervyn, that
the creature was odiously conceited. It pleased
me to know that I was able to live in two worlds
at the same time. Alas, how little did I dream
what the privilege would cost! My Other-Self,
after glancing superciliously around the room,
caught sight of the Ixioks at which I had been
working. I explained what they were.
“ ‘ Open a new set,’ It remarked with a laugh.
‘“For what reason ? ’ I inquired.
“‘Because,’lt replied, ‘your hoard of virtue
has vanished.’
“ ‘And how?’ I asked, incredulously.
“ ‘ Well,’ It answered coolly, ‘ a good deal of it
was used up in producing Me, and the remainder
I have spent.’
“ ‘ You’ve what ? ’ I cried aghast.
“ * Spent,’ It repeated, somewhat sullenly— ‘ you
don’t suppose I can live on nothing, do you ? ’
“ I was too disgusted and alsoastounded to reply.
At length, however, I pulled myself together and
observed, scornfully— * Pooh ! you have no objec-
tive existence ! You are only a hallucination ! ’
“‘Am I?’ It remarked drily. I need not tell
you, Dr. Mervyn, what means It used to convince
me that It was a reality. It is sufficient to say
that they were amply effective.
“‘And now,’ said my Other-Self, ‘I shall feel
obliged if you will exert yourself a little more in
doing good. I belong to a rather exclusive set of
Second-Stagers—Other-Selves of great philanthro-
pists, slum-workers, charity-organUesrs, and the
like —and the pace is very hot. Of qqurse, it’s ex-
pensive ; but then the company is very select, and
you would hardly like to know that your Other-
Self looked mean in such society. I shall probably
drop in upon you again before long. ’ And then It
floated out of the room. I burnt my account-
books that night and opened a new set. ”
# # #
“ ‘Since then I have had no peace. My
Other-Self has developed into a merciless tyrant,
and I have become its abject slave. It has plunged
me into the most frightful excesses of virtue —to
such an extent, indeed, that my friends begin to
think lam not sane. Fortunately, I have no rela-
tives. If I had they would certainly try to get an
injunction to restrain me from disposing of my
own property. I have become the prey of beg-
ging-letter writers and mendicants of every des-
cription. They seem to know that I dare not re-
fuse them, and have got into the habit of demand-
ing assistance as a right. On the few occasions
when I have summoned courage enough to rebel
against my thraldom, and have allowed a whole
week to go by without performing a single good
action, my Other-Self has appeared before me look-
ing so haggard and miserable that I have felt
like a criminal for having reduced It to such a
state. At these times It promises, with tears in
its eyes, to reform. But It never reforms. To do
It justice, I don’t think that It means ill. It has
no conscience, because, while I live, It has no
soul. I believe Its conduct to be simply the result
of a natural reaction against too much inherited
goodness. It is a spendthrift because It has not
had to earn what It spends. Nevertheless it
seems strange that such a reckless, irresponsible
being should be the product of an accumulation
of good deeds. The worst of the matter is that I
know that I have to be responsible for It. It is
continually drawing cheques upon me, if I may
use the term in such a connection, in the full con-
fidence that I will honor them. Only the other
night It dragged me out of my bed to prevent a
man in delirium tremens from cutting his throat.
He was a raving lunatic, and strong as seven bulls.
I barely succeeded in escaping from him with my
life, but I managed to divert his attention from
his own throat to mine until the police came and
carried him off to gaol. This good action was
hardly placed to my account in the Next Exist-
ence before it was drawn out by my Other-Self
and squandered. Have I never made an
heroic effort to rid myself of my Tyrant ?
Well, yes. I have tried a course of Vice, but
it was a dismal failure. At the end of it my
Other-Self, instead of coming to me looking
wretched and woe-begone, was simply radiant
with delight. My crimes had been booked to my
credit as virtues of the highest class, noble efforts
of self-sacrifice, because they had been committed
with so much loathing and disgust! So that
attempt to free myself only placed me in a worse
position than ever. Continual yielding on my
part has given my Other-Self such power over me
that now It is really the master and I am its slave.
If something is not done to check its extravagance
I shall have to commence my life in the next world
as a bankrupt. You now know my strange and
unhappy story, Dr. Mervyn.’
* » *
“I might have laughed,” said Dr. Mar-
garet, “though perhapssomewhatartificially, at the
Man’s story, if I had not been a Theosopliist and
believed that such things were possible, and if he
had not been so ternbly in earnest. As it was I
asked him in what way he thought my professional
services could help him, adding, with an amount
of candor which, I consider, was noble in a
struggling young practitioner, that I did not my-
self see how I could do him any good. Then I ven-
tured a little joke and said that if he had brought
his Other-Self with him, and could have induced
it to take some medicine I would have prescribed,
there might have been some .hope of his being
rescued from its tyranny, at least for a time. He
didn’t even smile, but gazed at me half-sadly,
half-severely, and remarked, ‘ Pray don’t do
injustice to your better nature by treating a
tragical subject with flippancy. I have come to
consult you because I feel, somehow, that you may
be able to assist me. What I require is an acces-
sion of vital power—or will-power- to enable me
to overcome the influence of my spiritual parasite.
Does the modern pharmacopoeia contain nothing
corresponding to those herbs known to the ancients
which were said to possess power over spirits ?’ ”
“ I said that if it did I was in ignorance of the
fact. His next remarks almost took my breath
away.
“ ‘ Then you must hypnotise me, ’he said. ‘ There
is no other remedy. And, when I am under the
spell, you must cause me to summon my Other-
Self, and make me disown and defy it, and all its
works. If you will do this I shall have the whip-
hand of it ever after, and, perhaps, know what
happiness means once more. ’ ”
“Of course, I replied that, even if I possessed the
necessary {tower, what he asked me was impossible
from a professional point of view. My rigorously
proper medical brethren would denounce me as a
charlatan, a Madam Mesmer, if they learnt that 1
was working cures by hypnotic suggestion.
“He looked so painfully disappointed that I felt
sorry for him, and said that I thought I could
help him in another way. And so, with some
affectation of mystery as to its ingredients, I
wrote out a prescription, and asked him to call
and let me know the result, after he had finished
the medicine. It was simply a strong nerve-tonic,
but described in a' kind of abbreviated doctor’s
latin, intelligible only to myself and my own
special chemist.
“You must assist the action of the drugs,” I
added, “by endeavouring to occupy your mind
with some subject which will make a strong
demand upon your attention—Browning’s ‘ Ring
and the Book,’ for instance.”
“He promised to obey my instructions implicitly
■* * *
“My curious patient paid me a second visit
about a week afterwards, looking paler and thinner
than ever. He said that he thought the medicine
had done him good, but “ The Ring and the Book, ”
which he had set himself seriously to understand,
had been too much for him. But he believed that
his Other-Self had lost a little of its power over
him. It had called upon him twice only during
the week, and he had had only one Orgy of Vir-
tue. I told him to keep to the medicine, and not
worry any more over “ The Ring and the Book,”
and he went away kicking comparatively cheerful.
“After this his calls became more frequent, and 1
noticed that he looked brighter and better on
each occasion, and had taken to wearing flowers
in his button-hole. But a gradual change in his
manner kept pace with this change in his appear-
ance. From being cool and self-possessed he
became nervous and shy. One day he called, and,
after talking on half-a-dozen subjects, went away
without saying a word about his malady. Then I
knew that ne was cured. I was rather soi’ry to
lose him as a patient. I had become quite inter-
ested in his case. That,” concluded Dr. Mar-
garet, “ is the story of my strangest experience as
a doctor. ”
“ But surely that isn’t all of the story ? ”
said one of the Bachelors of Medicine.
“ What became of the poor man’s Other-Self ? ”
the other Bachelor inquired.
“ You had better ask him ! Here he comes,
said Dr. Margaret.
Listening, they heard the sound of steps upon
the stairs and a voice singing in a rich, rolling bari-
tone the stave of “ Autolycus ”:
Jog on, jog on, the footpath-way,
And merrily hent the stile-a :
A merry heart goes all the (lay,
Your sad tires in a mile-a.
There was a chorus of exclamations. “You little
traitor ! you have been telling us about your hus-
band all the time.”
“Yes, dears. I married my patient. lie
doesn’t appear to be much worried about his
Other-Self now, docs he ?”
“No, indeed,” remarked Bachelor Kitty, “you
have given him a new interest in life.”
“ Yes,” replied Dr. Margaret dryly, “ and a new
commandment THOU SHALT HAVE NO
OTHER-SELF BUT ME.”
V. J. DALEY.
“ BULLETIN ” FASHIONS.
(By our own Fashion-plate Artist.)
The angel sleeve idea carried to a logical con-
ceusion. If angel SLEEVES, why not angel-s?
My Neighbor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My neighbor’s hat is black and tall.
How bright its silken sheen !
Compared with his, my home is small
And altogether mean ;
The hand he puts in mine oft-times
He does not care to soil,
And yet I praise in simple rhymes
The dignity of toil.
My neighbor’s buggy bears me out
For cool and pleasant drives,
He seldom lets me “ do a shout ”
When ’freshment time arrives.
He laughs with glee at all my quips ;
No nasty, pompous smirks,
But kindly smiles play round his lips
Although he never works.
My other neighbor is a smith ;
From early morn till e’en’
He earns an honest living with
Two horny paws unclean,
His adjectives are warm enough
To make a kettle boil,
Whilst he upholds, in accents gruff,
The dignity of toil.
My other neighbor has a ’eart
And soul within his breast;
A shilling he will gladly part
To help a mate distressed,
But as I do not want for food
And seem from trouble free,
He often growls: ‘ ‘ Twould do ’im good
To have to graft like me.”
Which is my neighbor of the pair ?
The smith, by fate denied
Those pleasures which 'tis sweet to share
With Jones? 1 can’t decide.
One man’s a credit to the soil.
The other toff’s a sloth
Who comes to me with corn and oil.
Which is my neighbor ? Both.
Melbourne.
EDMUND FISHER
Missing.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was tired of the myrtle forest,
And sick of the waratah—
That gleams in the lonely places,
And shines like a splendid star.
And the sound of the jay-birds calling
To a wearisome chant had grown—
For the voice of the bush is dreary,
And sad its dominant tone.
And ever, as time went slowly,
The longing became more keen
For a spell in the open country,
And a change to another scene.
So it came to pass ere the daylight
Had crept to the old bark nut,
That his swag was rolled for the journey,
And the door of his house was shut.
But whether he journeyed eastward —
Or whether he wandered west—
Is known to the bush-birds only,
And the angel that gave him rest.
And the place of his final resting
Shall a riddle remain unread,
Until, like the sea we read of —
The bush sh ill give up her dead !
U pper Derwent, Tas.
W.A.A.

"BEFORE WE COT THE DEEDS"
Cub selection adjoined a sTreep-run on the
Darling Downs, and boasted of few and scant im-
provements. A verandahless four-roomed slab-
hut standing out from a forest of box-trees, a
stock-yard, and six acres under barley were the
only evidence of settlement. A few horses—not
ours —sometimes grazed about, and occasionally a
mob of cattle—also not ours—cows with young
calves, steers, and an old bull or two, would stroll
around, chew the best legs of any trousers that
might be hanging on the log reserved as a clothes-
line, then leave in the night and be seen no more
for months —some of them never.
And yet we were always out of meat!
Dad was up the country earning a few pounds—
the corn drove him up. It didn’t bring what he
expected ; all we got out of it was a bag of flour—
I don’t know what the storekeeper got. Before
he left we put in the barley. Somehow, Dad
didn’t believe in sowing any more crops, he seemed
to lose heart; but Mother talked it over with him,
and when reminded that he soon would be en-
titled to the deeds he brightened up again and
worked. How he worked !
We had no plough, so old Anderson turned over
the six acres for us, and Dad gave him a pound an
acre—at least he was to send him the first six
pounds got up country. Dad sowed the seed ;
then he, Dan, and Dave yoked themselves to
a large dry bramble each and harrowed it in.
From the way they sweated it must have been
hard work. Sometimes they would sit down in
the middle of the paddock and “ spell,” but Dad
would say something about getting the deeds and
they’d start again.
A cockatoo-fence was round the barley ; and
wire-posts, a long distance apart, round tho grass-
paddock. We were to get wire to put in when
Dad sent the money; and apply for the deeds
when he came back. Things would be different
then, according to Dad, and the farm would be
worked properly. We would break up 50 acres,
build a barn, buy a reaper, ploughs, cornsheller,
get cows and good horses, and start two or three
ploughs. Meanwhile, if we (Dan, Dave and I)
minded the barley he was sure there’d be some-
thing got out of it.
« * *
Dad was now away about six weeks.
Travellers were passing by every day, and
there wasn’t one th a
didn’t want a little o.
something or other.
Mother used to ask them
if they had met Dad ?
None ever did until an
old grey man came along
and said he knew Dad
well—he had camped with
him one night and shared
a damper.' Mother was
very pleased and brought
him in. We had a kan-
garoo.-rat (stewed) for din-
ner that day. The girls
didn’t want to lay it on
the table at first, but
Mother said he wouldn’t
know what it was. The
traveller was very hungry
and liked it, and when
passing his plate the
second time for more, said
it wasn’t often that he got
any poultry.
He tramped on again,
and the girls were very
glad he didn’t know it
was a rat. But Dave
wasn’t so sure that he
didn’t know a rat from
a rooster, and reckoned he
hadn’t met Dad at all.
* * *
Tho seventh week
Dad came back. He
arrived at night, and the
lot of us had to get up to
find the hammer to knock
the peg out of the door
and let him in. He
Drought home <£ —no*
enough to get the wire w . ’ *ut he also brought
ahorse and saddle. He didn’t say if he bought
them. It was a bay mare, a grand animal for a
journey—so Dad said —and only wanted condi-
tion. Emelina, he called her. No mistake she
was a quiet mare. We put her where there was
good feed, but she wasn’t one that fattened on
grass. Birds took kindly to her—crows mostly
-and she. couldn’t go anywhere but a flock of
them accompanied her. Even when Dad used to
ride her (Dan or Dave never rode her) they used
to follow, and would fly on ahead to wait in a
tree and “caw ” when he was passing beneath.
* * *
One morning when Dan was digging
potatoes for dinner—splendid potatoes they were,
too, Dad said ; he had only once tasted sweeter
ones, but they were grown in a cemetery—he
found the kangaroos had been in the barley. We
knew what that meant, and that night made fires
round it, thinking to frighten them off, but didn’t
—mobs of them were in at daybreak. Dad
swore from the house at them, but they took no
notice ; and when he ran down, they just hopped
over the fence and sat looking at him. Poor Dad !
I don’t know if he was knocked up or if he didn t
know any more, but he stopped swearing and sat
on a stump looking at a patch of barley they had
destroyed, and shaking his head. Perhaps he was
thinking if he only had a dog ! We did have one
until he got a bait. Old Spring ! He was lying
under the table at supper-time when he took the
first fit, and what a- fright we got! He must have
reared before stiffening out, because he capsized
the table into Mother’s lap, and everything on it
smashed except the tin-plates and the pints. I he
lamp fell on Dad, too, and the melted fat scalded
his arm. Dad dragged
Bpring out and cut
off his tail and ears,
but he might as well
have taken off Ins
head.
« * *
Dad stood with
his back to the fire
and Mother was nut-
ting a stitch in his
trousers. “There’s
nothing for it but
to watch them at
n ight, ” he was say ’; ig,
when old And r. on
appeared and a ked
“if he could have
those few pounds.”
Dad asked Mother if
she had any money
in the house ? Of
course she hadn’t.
Then he told Ander-
son he would let him
have it when he got
the deeds. Anderson
’eft, and Dad sat on
the edge of the sofa auJ seemed to be count
ing the grains on a corn-cob that he lifted from the
floor, while Mother sat looking at a kangaroo-tail
on the table and didn’t notice the cat drag it off.
At last Dad said, “Ah, well!—it won’t be long
now, Ellen, before we have the deeds !”
* * *
We now took it in turns to watch the
barley. Dan and the two girls the first half,
and Dad, Dave and 1 the second. Dad
always slept in his clothes, and he used
to think some nights that the others came in
before time. It was terrible going out, half-
awake, to tramp round that paddock from fire to
fire and from hour to hour, shouting and yelling.
And how we used to long for daybreak ! When-
ever we sat down quietly together for a few
minutes we would hear the dull thud ! thud ! thud!
—the kangaroo’s footstep.
We each carried a kerosene-tin at last, slung
like a kettledrum, and belted it with a waddy—■
Dad’s idea. He himself manipulated an old bell
that he had found on a bullock’s grave, and made
a splendid noise with it.
It was a hard struggle, but we succeeded in
saving the bulk of the barley, and cut it down
with a scythe and three reaping-hooks. The girls
helped to bind it, and Jimmy Mulcahy carted it
in return for three days’ binding Dad put in for
him. The stack wasn’t built 24 hours when a
score of somebody’s crawling cattle ate their way up
to their tails into it. We took the hint and pu ta
sapling fence round it.
* * *
Again Dad decided to go up country for
a while. He caught Emelina after breakfast,
rolled up a blanket, told us to watch the stack,
and started.
The crows followed.
We were having dinner. Dave said, “ Listen.”
We listened, and it seemed as though all the
crows and other feathered demons of the wide
bush were engaged in a mighty scrimmage.
“Dad’s back,” Dan said, and rushed out in the
ato A jp 3 B<>
lead of a stampede.
Emelina was back, anyway, with the swag on,
but Dad wasn’t. We caught her, and Dave
pointed to white spots all over the saddle, and
said —“ Hanged if they haven’t been ridin’ her! ”
meaning the crows.
Mother got anxious, and sent Dan to see what
had happened. Dan found Dad, with his shirt
off, at a pub. on the main road, wanting to fight
the pubhoan for a hundred pounds, but couldn’t
fiersuade him to come home. Two men brought him
mme that night on a sheep-hurdle, and he gave
up the idea of going away.
After all, the barley turned out well—
there was a good price that year, and we were
able to run two wires round the paddock.
One day a bulky Govt, letter came—Dad looked
surprised and pleased, and how his hand trembled
as he broke the seal! “ The Deeds !”he said, and
all of us gathered round to look at them. Dave
thought they were like the inside of a bear-skin
covered with writing.
Dad said he would ride to town at once, and
went for Emelina.
“Couldn’t y’ find her, Dad?” Dan said, seeing
him return without the mare.
Dad cleared his throat, but didn’t answer.
Mother asked him.
“ Yes, 1 found her,” he said, slowly, “dead."
The crows had got her at last.
He wrapped the deeds in a piece of rag and
walked.
There was nothing, scarcely, that he didn’t send
out from town, and Jimmy Mulcahy and old
Anderson have many and many times since then
borrowed our dray.
Now Dad regularly curses the deeds every
mail-day, and wishes to Heaven he had never got
them.
Queensland.
STEELE RUDD.
Hogan’s Cow; or, Ireland in
Australia.
[FO R THE BULLETIN.]
The still bush lay that summer’s day all splashed
with brown and yellow,
And gleamed and glowed as Hogan rode in haste
to “ Bally-hello.”
His mare was wet with running sweat, her sides
spurred raw and gory ;
Tor Irish breed with sword and steed goes hard to
grog or glory.
Nor need I tell, who know them well, their virtues
and their vices—
His thirst was such ’twould melt at touch Mount
Kosciusko’s ices.
With some thick curse (unfit for verse) he took
the slip-rails standing,
His well-bred mare right “ fair and square ” upon
her legs safe landing.
Then down he came with face aflame and shouts
of Celtic greeting—
Good Irish screech and Irish speech in words of
welcome meeting.
’Twas Sabbath day ; the house of Shea, to its
stout host’s elation,
Ould Father Tim (soft rest to him !) had chosen for
a “ station. ’
Trom range and flat at word of that they came in
divers fashions,
Repenting long, with grief right strong, their
follies and hot passions.
By saints they swore to thieve no more, and
harder yet, be jabers,
Each Celtic wight vowed not to fight with his
adjacent neighbors—
(“To taste no drink,” some time I think poor
Father Tim ne’er pressed them :
He’d found full long temptation strong, as good
“ pot heen ” would best them I
The priest had gone, but lingered on their nags
amongst the clover,
Trom east and west, by Shea’s request, a score of
folks and over.
The bush scent sweet with smell of meat, both
salt or fresh (or tainted),
Was mixed somewhat; her oven got so warm
“the Missus” fainted.
Around the board a festive norde of Tooles,
O’Neills and Shannons,
And Ryans red, in peace broke bread with
Burkes, Malones and Gannons.
From Cassins blue a classic hue) took “ tay ”
sweet colleens, frisky,
Whilst drank from mugs, jars, saucers, jugs, the
male contingent, whisky.
They talked of “ Home ” —o’er leagues of foam the
exiles’ thoughts went gliding,
Whilst rough eye-lids of “Pats” and “Bids”
showed tears that bore no hiding.
“ God Save the Green” they sang between deep
s.ghs and swigs of liquor,
Lamenting so that in its flow both grief and grog
ran quicker.
Then one O’Dowd, to cheer the crowd, his con-
certina started,
With music sad till lass and lad were well-nigh
broken -hearted.
If poor Tom Moore could rise, I’m sure, though
dust and Death enfold him,
His shade ’twould cheer to find how dear all Irish-
men yet hold him,
W h it grief still flows o’er Tom’s ‘ ‘ Last Rose ” and
whisky by the gallon
Doth drench in joy his “ Minstrel Boy ” and flood
“ Sweet Innisfallen !”
Oh ! when they meet in camp or street, fair Erin’s
sons and daughters,
There, too, will be in grief or glee, a “ Meeting of
the Waters.”
So Hogan drew the rein and threw the saddle
from Gay Lady,
Then midst the clans stood wringing hands of
Burke, Malone, and Brady,
i A “stirrup-cup” they filled him up of stout
potheen and fiery,
Ere he might loose his tongue to news or answer
one enquiry.
They piled a plate with spuds and “mate,” and
sate him by the table,
Whilst Missus Shea brewed “ pot o’ tay ”as strong
as ocean cable,
And need I tell that Hogan fell upon the fare
they bore him,
That “mate” and spuds with dull, dead thuds
went quickly down before him ?
“I’m late,” said he, “for Mass, you see” —(his
brows here bent together)—
“ Thim roads, bedad, is worse nor bad beyant
since last wet weather,
But, by the post, what kep’ me most ” —(again he
scowled like thunder) —
“My brindled cow is shtole 1 . . I vow by all
above and undher
That if Oi can Oi’ll lay the man that’s got the
bate in pickle,
And whe iOi ketcl. 1 1 ■ tarnal wretch his hide, by
troth, Oi’ll tickle ! ’
Kight just his cause, and loud applause met
Hogan’s peroration,
Tor each believed —as each had thieved from each
of stock and station.
In those old days the bush had ways, peculiar
from our standing—
That they should take who could and fake (when-
e’er ’twas safe) the branding.
You stole my beast? Well, west or east next
night I’d ride to “ borrow ”
A beast in lieu of that I knew you’d salted ere the
morrow.
’Twas all fair play, by night and day each “ pil-
fered ” from the other,
And sire from son his mob would run, and
brother stole from brother.
Thus Hogan told his tale and rolled great oaths
about the brindle,
And ate and swore and told it o’er .vhat time the
food did dwindle ;
He drank and ate ! “ Tis tindher mate,” and,
Mrs. Shea, Oi’m grateful
Tor such a feed. Aye, faix ! Indeed, Oi’ll take
another plateful.
Don’t shtop the fun ye’ve just begun ; kapeup the
choone there, Denny !
Malone ! Whurroo ! Good luck to you—meself,
Oi don’t get any ! ”
Now, Mrs. Shea, alas to say, though kinder hand
ne’er fed one,
By some hard fate got on his pi tte a white hair
and a red one I
Which Hogan saw, and straight his jaw dropped
like a dying fish’s
While fork and knife in his soul’s strife went
clatt’nng ’midst the dishes.
Tor quick as thought those hairs had brought unto
his mind a notion
That in his breast (almost at rest) made manifest
commotion.
“ ’Tis Daisy’s hide,’ at length he cried, tobearthe
strain unable ;
And as he spoke the plate he broke in splinters on
the table —•
“ Oi’d know the hair, though sarved up there, of
her that Oi did cherish,
Oi’ll sarch,” he swore, as out he tore, “}o r stack
of hides or perish ! ! ”
Alas for Shea, what could he say when high o’er-
head ’twas flourished
The brand cut out but not a doubt the hide that
Hogan “ nourished ? ”
In truth to tell the fearsome yell emitted by the
latter
Tor some slight space paled Shea’s red face and
stilled the growing clatter.
“Ye Orange thief!” he roared with grief and
savage Celtic passion,
And added to the words a few choice epithets in
fashion—
“ Oi’ll see ye hung !” Thereat he flung the hide
in Shea’s broad features.
“ Take that from me, ’tis fit for ye who robs his
fellow creatures !”
They fought in pairs with legs of chairs and sticks
and odds and ends there,
And each fresh blow marked one more foe ’midst
those who had been friends there ;
Engaged all round in open ground, a fence-rail ftr
shillelah,
Not oft you’d see a fight as free nor one that went
so gaily.
Why, Mick Malone broke Burke’s skull-bone first
crack and laid him gasping,
But with the same a stirrup came on Mick’s own
headpiece rasping ;
Then Pat O’Nale got more fence-rail than he had
strength to carry;
And Thady Kane tried all in vain a pitchfork
thrust to parry.
Just when ’twas done a scarlet sun went down
great clouds of red in,
Where Hogan got the Sheas’ big pot to beat its
owner’s head in.
Then, as the night closed up that fight our hero
bold rode through it,
His saddle wet (but not with sweat), and Daisy's
hide strapped to it I
Now as they ride across the wide, rough table-
lands and ranges,
Where bushman brown from up and down with
bushman yarns exchanges ;
Trom Braidwood side to Lachlan - tide, from
Bathurst to the Bogan,
They never fail to tell the tale of that famed fight
and Hogan.
Go where you will o’er plain and hill, by farm and
far selection,
They don’t forget, it lingers yet, a pleasant recol-
lection.
Aye ! where clear creeks from blue hill peaks flow
down thro’ fields of clover,
Each sttmmer day amongst the hay their children
play it over !
Sydney, April, 1895.
O.W.L.

THE MERINO SHEEP
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The prosperity of Australia is absolutely
based on a beast —the merino sheep. If all the
sheep in the country were to die, the big banks
would collapse like card houses, the squatting
securities, which are their backbone, being gone.
Business would perish, and the money we owe
to England would be as hopelessly lost to that'
nation as if we were a South American state.
The sheep, and the sheep alone, keeps us going.
On the back of this beneficent creature we all live.
Knowing this, people have got the impression that
the merino sheep is a gentle, bleating animal that
gets its living without trouble to anybody, and
comes up every year to be shorn with a pleased
smile upon its amiable face. It is my purpose here,
as one having experience, to exhibit the merino
sheep in its true light, so that the public may
know wluvt kind of brute they are depending on.
• * #
And first let us give him what little
credit is his due. No one can accuse him
of being a ferocious animal. No one could
ever say that a sheep attacked him without
provocation ; though there is an old bush story of
a man who was discovered in the act of killing a
neighbour’s wether. “Hallo!” said the neigh-
bour. “What’s this? Killing my sheep ! What
have you got to say for yourself ?’’ “ Yes,” said
the man, with an air of virtuous indignation. “ I
am killing your sheep. I’ll kill any man’s sheep
that bites me !” But as a rule the merino refrains
from using his teeth on people, and goes to work
in another way.
* # *
The truth is that the merino sheep is a
dangerous monomaniac, and his one idea is—to
ruin the man who owns him. With this object in
view he will display a talent for getting into
trouble and a genius for dying that are
almost incredible. If a mob of sheep see
a bush fire closing round them, do they run
away out of danger? Not at all; they rush
round and round in a ring till the fire burns them
up. If they are in a river-bed, with a howling
flood coming down, they will stubbornly refuse to
cross three indies of water to save them-
selves. Dogs and men may bark and shriek,
but the sheep won’t move. They will wait there
till the flood comes and drowns them all, and then
their corpses go down the river on their backs with
their feet in the air. A mob of sheep will crawl
along a road slowly enough to exasperate a snail,
but let a lamb get away from the mob in a bit of
rough country, and a racehorse can’t head him
back again. If sheep are put into a big paddock
with water in three corners of it, they will reso-
lutely crowd into the fourth corner and die of
thirst. When sheep are being counted out at a
gate, if a scrap of bark be left on the ground in
the gateway, they will refuse to step over it until
dogs and men have sweated and toiled and sworn
and “heeled ’em up,” and “spoke to ’em,” and
fairly jammed them at it. Then the first one will
gather courage, rush at the fancied obstacle, spring
over it about six feet in the air, and dart away.
The next does exactly the same, but jumps a bit
higher. Then comes a rush of them following one
another in wild bounds like antelopes, until one
“over-jumps himself” and alights on his head—a
performance which nothing but a sheep could
compass. This frightens those still in the yard,
and they stop running out, and the dogging and
shrieking and hustling and tearing have to be gone
through all over again. This on a red-hot day, mind
you, with clouds of blinding dust about, with the
yolk of wool irritating your eyes, and with, perhaps,
three or four thousand sheep to put through.
The delay throws out the man who is counting,
and he forgets whether he left off at 45 or 95.
The dogs, meanwhile, take the first chance to slip
over the fence and hide in the shade somewhere.
Then there are loud whistlings and oaths, and calls
for Rover and Bluey, and at last a dirt-begrimed
man jumpsover the fence, unearths a dog and hauls
him back to work by the ear. The dog sets to
barking and heeling-’em-up again, and pretends
that he thoroughly enjoys it, but he is looking
out all the time for another chance to “clear.”
And this time lie won’t be discovered in a hurry.
# # #
To return to our muttons. There is a
well-authenticated story of a shipload of sheep
being lost once, because an old ram jumped over-
board into the ocean, and all the rest followed
him. No doubt they did, and were proud to do it. A
sheep won’t go through an open gate on his own
responsibility, but he would gladly and proudly
follow another sheep through the red-hot portals
of Hades : and it makes no difference whether
the leader goes voluntarily, oris hauled struggling
and kicking and fighting every inch of the way.
For pure, sodden stupidity there is no animal like
the merino sheep. A lamb will follow a bullock-
dray drawn by 16 bullocks and driven by a pro-
fane “colonial” with a whip, under the impression
that this aggregate monstrosity is his mother. A
ewe never knows her own lamb by sight, and
apparently has no sense of colour. She can
recognise her own lamb's voice half a mile off
among a thousand other voices apparently exactly
similar; bub when she gets within five yards of
her lamb she starts to smell all the lambs in reach,
including the black ones, though her own may be
a white lamb. The fiendish resemblance which
one sheep bears to another is a great advantage to
them in their struggles with their owners. It
makes them more difficult to draft out of a strange
flock, and much harder to tell when any are
missing.
Concerning this resemblance between sheep,
there is a story told of a fab old Murrum-
bidgee squatter who gave a big price for a
famous ram called, say, Sir Oliver. He took
a friend out one day to inspect Sir Oliver,
and overhauled that animal with a most impressive
air of sheep-wisdom. “Look here,” he said, “atthe
fineness of the wool. See the serrations in each
thread of it. See the density of it. Look at the
way his legs and belly are clothed—-he’s wool all
over, that sheep. Grand animal, grand animal!”
Then they went and had a drink, and the old
squatter said, “Now, I’ll show you the difference
between a champion ram and a second-rater.” So
he caught a ram and pointed out his defects.
“ See here—not half the serrations that other
sheep had. No density of fleece to speak of.
Bare-bellied as a pig, compared with Sir Oliver.
Not that this isn’t a fair sheep, bub he’d be dear
at one-tenth Sir Oliver’s price. By the way,
Johnson” (to his overseer, “what ram is this?”
“ That, sir,” replied the astounded functionary—
-11 that’s Sir Oliver, sir !” And so it was.
• # •
There is another kind of sheep in Aus-
tralia, as great a curse in his own way as the
merino—namely, the cross-bred or half-merino-
half-Leicester animal. The cross-bred will get
through, under, or over any fence you like to pub
in front of him. He is never satisfied on his
owner’s run, but always thinks other people’s runs
must be better, so he sets off to explore. He
will strike a course, say, south-east, and so long
as the fit takes him he will keep going south-east
through all obstacles, rivers, fences, growing crops
anything. The merino relies on passive resist-
ance for his success; the cross-bred carries the
war into the enemy’s camp, and becomes a living
curse to his owner day and night. Once there was
a man who was induced in a weak moment to
buy 20 cross-bred rams, and from that hour
the hand of Fate was upon him. They gob
into all the paddocks they shouldn’t have been
in. They scattered themselves all over the
run promiscuously. They got into the cultivation-
paddock and the vegetable-garden at their own sweet
will. And then they took to roving. In a body
they visited the neighbouring stations, and played
havoc with the sheep all over the district. The
wretched owner was constantly getting fiery
letters from his neighbours : “ Your rams
are here. Come and take them away at
once,” and he would have to go off nine or ten
miles to drive them home. Any man who
has tried to drive rams on a hot day knows what
purgatory is. He was threatened with actions for
trespass for scores of pounds damages every
week. He tried shutting them up in the sheep-
yard. They got out and went back to the garden.
Then he gaoled them in the calf-pen. Out again
and into a growing crop. Then he set a boy to
watch them, but the boy went to sleep, and they
were four miles away across country before he got
on to their tracks. At length, when they
happened accidentally to be at home on their
owner’s run there came a huge flood. His sheep
mostly merinoes, bad plenty of time to get on to
high ground and save their lives, but, of course,
they didn’t, and they were almost all drowned.
The owner sat on a rise above the waste of waters
and watched the dead animals go by. He was
a ruined man. His hopes in life were gone.
But he said, “Thank God, those rams are drowned,
anyhow.” Just as he spoke there was a splashing
in the water, and the twenty rams solemnly swam
ashore and ranged themselves in front of him.
They were the only survivors of thousands of
sheep. He broke down utterly, and was taken to
an asylum for insane paupers. The cross-breds had
fulfilled iheir destiny.
*' * #
The cross-bred drives his owner out of his
mind, but the merino ruins his man with greater
celerity. Nothing on earth will kill cross-breds,
while nothing will keep merinoes alive. If they
are put on dry saltbush country they die of
drought. If they are put on damp, well-watered
country they die of worms, fluke, and foot-rot.
They die in the wet seasons and they die in the
dry ones. The hard, resentful look which you
may notice on the faces of all bushmen comes
from a long course of dealing with the merino
sheep. It is the merino sheep which dominates
the bush, and which gives to Australian literature
its melancholy tinge, and its despairing pathos.
The poems about dying boundary-riders, and
lonely graves under mournful she-oaks, are the
direct outcome of the authors’ too close association
with that soul-destroying animal, the merino
sheep. A man who could write anything cheerful
after a day in the drafting-yards would be a freak
of nature.
THE BANJO.
Know Thyself.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The of old spake words of truth,
And yet his golden speech untimely ended.
For when he wisely told each maid and youth
“ Go, know thyself! ” he might have haply
blended
That wise instruction with imperious order
To secret keep their characters’ cracked delf.
For man should give the news to no recorder,
But know himself and keep it to himself.
RANDOLPH BEDFOKD
TWO OCENA
TRAMPS
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I was listening to those charming musicians, the
convict band, playing in Noumea, and saw in the
crowd a man I knew—more, an old friend, S .
The recognition was mutual and pleasing to both.
We had not met for six years. He was then chief
officer of a China steamer; now he was captain of
a big tramp steamer that had called in to load
nickel ore. “Who,” I exclaimed, “would ever
have thought of meeting you here ?”
He laughed and replied : “1 came with a pur-
pose. You remember Miss ,to whom I was
engaged in Sydney ?”
I nodded, expecting from the sparkle in his
dark brown eye that I was going to hear a little
gush about her many wifely qualities.
“ Well, I was in Sydney three times after I saw
you. We were to be married as soon as I got a
command. Two years ago I was there last. She
had got married. Yes, wrote me a letter saying
she knew my calmer judgment would finally
triumph over my anger—she had accepted a good
offer, and although I might be nettled, perhaps, at
first, yet she was sure my good sense would
applaud her decision in marrying a man who,
although she could never love him as she loved
me, was very rich. But she would always look
forward to meeting me again. That was all.”
“ Hard lines,” I said.
“ My dear boy, I thought that at first, when her
letter knocked me flat aback. But I got over it
and I swore I would pay her out. And I came to
this den of convicts to do it, and I did it—yester-
day. She is here.”
“Here?” I said.
So, then, I learnt the rest of Captain S ’s
story. A year after his lady-love had jilted him
ho received a letter from her in England. She
was in sad trouble, she said. Her husband, a
Victorian official, was serving five years for em-
bezzlement. Her letter was suggestive of a desire
to hasten to the “protection ” of her sailor lover.
She wished, she said, that her husband were dead.
But dead or alive she would always hate him.
S merely acknowledged her letter and sent
her £25. In another six months he got a letter
from Fiji. She was a governess there, she said, at
£75 a year. Much
contrition and love,
also, in this letter.
S sent another
£25, and remarked
that he would see
her soon. Fate one
day sent him to take
command ofa steamer
in Calcutta bound to
Fiji with coolies,
thence to Noumea to
load nickel ore. And
all the way out across
the tropics S ’s
heart was leaping at
the thought of seeing
his lost love—and
telling her that he
hated her for her
black, frozen trea-
chery.
As soon as he had
landed his coolies he
set about locating
the family with whom
she lived. No one
knew them, but a
planter explained
matters: “I know
the lady for whom
you enquire, but she
doesn’t go by that
name. Ask anyone
about Miss , the
barmaid. She has
gone to New Cale-
donia.”
He asked, and
learned that she was
well - known ; and
S wondered why
she had brought her
beauty to such a
climate as that of
Fiji when it would
have paid iier so much
better to parade it in
Melbourne.
The evening of the day on which his steamer
arrived at Noumea a man brought him a letter.
He showed it to me ;
My darling Will. Thank God you have come, lor
surely you have come for me—my heart tells me so. For
God’s sake wait on board for me. I will come at eight.
To live in this place is breaking my heart. Ever yours,
She came. He stood her kisses passively but
gave none in return, until she asked him to kiss
her. “ When you are my wife,” he said evasively.
And then—l believe she must have loved him—
she burst out into passionate sobs as she fell at his
feet in the quiet cabin, and told him of her debased
life in Fiji. “ But, as God hears me, Will, that
is all past since your last letter. I was mad. I
loved money and did not care how I got it. I left
Fiji to come here, intending to return to Aus-
tralia. But, Will, dear Will, if it is only to throw
me overboard, take me away from this hell upon
earth. For your sake, Will, I have resisted them
here, although I suffer daily, hourly, torture and
insult. I have no money and lam afraid to die
and end my sufferings.”
Captain S , speaking calmly and slowly,
placed money in her hand and said, “You must
not see me again till the day I am ready for sea.
Then bring your luggage and come on board.”
With a smothered sob bursting from her, despite
the joy in her heart, the woman turned and left
him. Then S went up to the oaf 6 Palais and
played billiards with a steady hand.
There was a great number of people on board to
see Captain S—— away. Presently a boat came
alongside, and a young lady with sweet red lips
and shiny hair ascended to the deck.
“Ho, ho 1” said a French officer to S , “ and
so you are taking away the fair one who won’t
look at us poor exiles of Nouvelle.”
With a timid smile and fast-beating heart the
woman gained the quarter-deck. In front of her
stood the broad-shouldered Captain S , cold,
impassive, and deadly pale, with a cruel joy in
his breast.
The woman stood still. There was something
so appalling in that set white face before her that
her slight frame quivered with an unknown dread.
And then the Captain spoke, in slow, measured
words that cut her to her inmost soul.
“ Madam, I do not take passengers !”
No answer. Only short, gasping breaths as she
steadied her hand on the rail.
And then, turning to one of the Frenchmen :
“M. , will you request this—t- is lady to go on
shore ? She is known to me as a woman of in-
famous reputation in Fiji. I cannot for a moment
entertain the idea of having such a person on
board my ship.”
Before the shuddering creature fell a man caught
her, and then she was placed in the boat and taken
ashore. Of course some of the Frenchmen
thought it right to demand an explanation from
S , who said :
“ I’ve none to give, gentlemen. If any of you
want to fight me, well and good, although I don’t
like quarrelling over a pavement-woman. Besides,
I rather think you’ll find that the lady will now be
quite an acquisition to you.”
But S ’s revenge was not complete. He had
previously arranged matters with his engineer,
who presently came along and announced an acci-
dent to the machinery—the steamer would b"
delayed a couple of days. You see, S wanted
to see her again—as he told me.
“It was a cruel thing,” I said.

The Worthington Tamping
Engine Co.
Messrs. Henry R. Worthington’s Works at
Brooklyn, New York, with the foundry at Eliza-
beth Port,Ne w Jersey—the largest of the world’s hy-
draulic works exclusively manufacturing pumping
machinery—employ 2000 men and turn out 30 to 40
pumps daily ; 10,000 to 12,000 yearly. The out-
put up to January, ’95, was 85,000, and the regular
stock exceeds 2000. The firm’s branch offices and
agencies are established in every worthy trade
centre of civilisation. Those outside the United
States are controlled by the Worthington Engine
Company of Queen Victoria-st., London, E.C. —
the big European and colonial branch company of
the great American firm.
• # *
In 1884, the eminent engineering firm, Messrs.
James Simpson and Co., Ltd., Grosvenor-road,
London, arranged with Messrs. Henry R. Worth-
ington to take up the manufacture of the larger-
sized Worthington engines. This fact is the
strongest testimony to the correctness of the
Worthington system of pumping. For Messrs.
Simpson and Co. are a century old, and have
devoted themselves so largely to the manufacture
of pumping engines that they always stood as the
leading English firm for this class of manufacture.
Their famous
type, the Com-
pound Rotative
Beam Bucket and
Plunger Pumping
Engine is known
world-wide as the
Simpson En-
gine, nevertheless they abandoned their own type
and adopted the superior Worthington.
w * *
Both the European and Colonial Branch Com-
pany of Messrs. Henry R. Worthington and
James Simpson and Co., Limited, are represented
in Australia by the Worthington Pumping Engine
Co.’s Head Australian Branch at 32 Clarence-
street, Sydney, and by this branch’s agencies in
all the other colonies. All these display a large
and splendid stock comprising every variety and
capacity of pumps, delivering from 200 gallons to
45,000 gallons hourly, and suitable for heads of
from 50 feet to 700 feet.
• • •
In the United States Messrs. Henry R Worth-
ington have equipped over 1000 cities with water-
works pumping machinery. The greater portion
of the crude oil which comes through many miles
of pipes in America is pumped by Worthington
Engines, for they work so smoothly and steadily
that engines of 400 h.p., working against a
pressure of 18001 b. per square
inch, can be employed without
bursting the mains.
* # •
At the Chicago Exhibition the
Worthington Engines presented
the most striking exhibit of
pumping machinery ever seen,
and pumped 70.000,000 gallons
every 24 hours. This grand ex-
hibit represented £40,000.
# * •
In England, Europe, Asia, and
Africa Messrs. Simpson and Co.
have supplied Worthington
Engines with magnificent success.
Seventy per cent, of London’s im-
mense water-supply is handled by
Worthington Engines, and the
Worthington Engines at the
County Council Sewage Pumping
Stations, Abbey Mills, put
through 18,000,000 gallons of
sewage daily. At Rotterdam
large Worthington Engines are
e nployed on the sewage, and
Rottei dam has given three i epeat
orders. The pumping at the
Johannesburg Water Works,
South Africa, is done by Wor-
thineton Engines. At Bombay
W orthington Engines manage
60,000,000 gallons of sewage every
24 hours. At Osaka, Japan, at
Hongkong, and at all the prin-

Turning now to
Australia, the re-
maining continent, the Worthington Engines
employed by the Sydney Metropolitan Board
of Water Supply and Sewage at Crown-
street Pumping Station have a capacity
of 12,000,000 and 8,000,000 gallons per day
respectively. The Board certifies that they
perform their work economically and most satis-
factorily. These Crown-st. engines are well-worth
inspecting, because they are the largest installation
of pumping machinery in the Southern Hemis-
phere.
A large compound pressure pump has recently
been supplied to the Sydney
Stevedoring and Wool Dump-
ing Co. at Moore’s wharf. This
pump is working against a
pressure of over 8001 b. on the
sq. inch, and performs its duties
with remarkable satisfaction.
A similar type of pump has
lately been sent to two Queens-
land mines to drain shafts 800
feet deep, doing the work in
one lift. Different contractors
on the Melbourne Sewage
Works express great satisfac-
tion at the performance of the
Worthington Pumps used in
draining different shafts ; two
Worthington pressure pumps are
used to force the Greathead
shields forward in the tunnels
of one contract, “Pumping
against a pressure of up to
25001 b. to sq. inch through
feed pipes nearly 2000 feet
long.'’ The Shepparton-Urban
Water Works Trust consider
that the Worthington Pumpsare
superior to all other types
which they have tried; they
pump double the quantity
another engine did and use
one-half the fuel. The Mayor
of Balranald says : “The Wor-
thington Pump used on water
supply have given every satis-
faction the last 10 years, and have
cost only =£lo in repairs.” Two complete Worth-
ington pumping plants, intended for the Water
Supply of Kalgoorlie, have just been shipped for
Westralia. The order came by telegram from the
Westralian Government, and the pumps were
shipped within five days of receiving it.
• • •
The Worthington Engine is the world’s recog-
nised standard of pumping machinery. It is the
original Duplex pump and one of many proofs of
the esteem it commands is its host of would-be
imitators ; the enormous number of duplex pumps
now on the market as “ Worthington System,”
“Worthington Type,” “Improved Worthing-
ton,” &c., &c. The real Worthington, however,
bears the trade-mark on a small plate securely
attached, and this fact cannot be too forcibly im-
pressed upon the minds of intending purchasers.
The Company has received a good many com-
plaints from purchasers of bogus Worthington
pumps.
• * *
The Worthington Company aim at quality—that
is true cheapness. Their engines are the best that
can be produced at the price, and » ith the evi-
dence of five continents to support their merits,
they intend to uphold their name in Australia and
to defy criticism.

in the future as in the past, and with every prospect
of securing results which have heretofore been as
gratifying to the Company as to that portion of the

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback— the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defeet the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

A Love-Song up to Date .
(THE NEW WOMAN SINGS.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thou art a vision of delight,
0 love, to me 1
In blissful dreams, by day and night,
Thy face I see.
My strong soul trembles at the sight,
And yearns for thee !
And yearns—and yearns—for thee.
If I could thy hard heart beguile,
Fair were my days.
0 press me to that heart awhile —
1 wear no stays !
1 love thy sweetly silly smile,
Thy foolish ways.
Thy foolish —foolish—ways.
The hairs of thy moustache, dear Fred,
My heart have speared.
0 let me lay my throbbing head,
My brow sin-seared,
Upon thy chest, and stroke thy red,
Sweet pointed beard !
Sweet pointed—pointed—beard.
This heart of mine that sounds the charge
’Gainst man’s deceit,
Doth, like the billow on the marge
Of Ocean, beat
To hear the music of thy large,
And lovely feet.
And love-ly—love-ly—feet.
1 see thee sometimes in eclipse :
Mine eyes I wipe ;
In public-houses taking nips
(Sweet, must thou swipe ?)
A cutty pipe between thy lips !
O happy pipe 1
0 hap-py—hap-py—pipe !
1 am the Oak, and thou the Vine—
O cling to me 1
Around my form thine arms entwine !
Why bashful be ?
Come, foolish, flutt’ring sweetheart mine
Sit on my knee !
Sit on my—on my —knee.
Vic.
C.R.
The Bulletin publishes the following as a

MARRIAGES.
PULLEINE —FOUCARX.—August 27, at the Church
of Canons Ashby, the ancestral estate of Sir Henry
Dryden, Bart., by the Right Rev. Lord Bishop of
Richmond, assisted by the Rev. R. Pulleine, uncle
and cousin of the bridegroom, and the Rev. A.
Teblett, Sir Henry Dryden’s private chaplain, Mr.
Henry Percy Pulleine, 24th Regiment South Wales
Borderers, only son of the late Lieutenant-Colonel
Pulleine, 241 h Regiment, to Alice Maud May
Fouoart, only daughter of Dr. Foucart, late of
Sydney.
English society papers described the new
Duchess of Marlborough (Vanderbilt’s daughter)
with copious detail, such as age (18), height, color
of hair, eyes, etc., shape of nose, weight, length
of hand, foot, etc., size of mouth, ears, head—
every item of description, in short, that is taken
of criminals. Her teeth are scheduled as “ white,
regular, and well-kept. She cycles, has a marriage
dot of £2,000,000, and a prospective fortune of
£5,000,000.
Send to your friends in England, America
and the Cape The Bulletin’s history of the Dean
case. Price, 6d. ; posted, 7d.
At Melb. Cup-time. A train full of book-
makers, and the conversation turned to the works
of Charles Dickens. “Is it a fact that Dickens
did not finish his last work?” said Lazarus
Solomon. “Oh, yes,” said Elijah Cohen, “it
was a double-book —the two Cups. He died
before he got round ! ”
Mr. H. J. Samuell, the well-known music-
publisher, is issuing on 7tli December No. 2 of
Violet’s Musical Album. It contains 15 songs
and pieces, and is about the most liberal shillings-
worth of music yet offered in Australia,*

Item from the Wilson divorce-case (Melb.):
Further examined, the petitioner admitted that he
frequently swore ai his wife and called her names. On
one occasion he pushed her, and she fell down. He never
ran down the yard after her and threatened to cut her
throat. He would not swear he had not once run after
her with a knife, but she had done the same to him.
When they lived at Carlton he could not have pulled her
around the yard by the hair of her head, as she then had
very little hair on her head.
This certainly reads as if he had used up all her
hair pulling her round the yard at their previous
residence, but no doubt that wasn’t what he
meant. Anyhow, the moral is that women should
wear their hair short.
The man who wears a silk hat in Aus-
tralian summer-time is the least enviable of little
boys at Manly, but, if he will do it, G. H. Smith
and Son recommend their light silk toppers, in
the latest shapes, as the best possible alleviation
of his woe. Their felts, and terais, and straws,
and all the other varieties of the implement which
half humanity, in its wisdom, has seen fit to dab
its top end into, are also claimed as specially
worth inspection; and G. H. Smith and Son’s
claim invariably pans out a solid oz. to the dish.*
For coughs, colds you can’t beat Colemane
and Sons Eucalypte Extract.*
A correspondent : Earth-worms grow to
a considerable size on the Arthur River, Tas._ I
remember seeing a long string of them cooking
before the fire at a Chinaman’s camp in the gold
days. “ What you call him?” asked a Chow, hold-
ing up one of the dainty morsels for inspection;
“Those are worms, John.” “ Wolums ! Oh!
him very good chow-chow!” Then gobble-
gobble-gobble, as the reptile slowly disappeared.
The writer of the Children’s Column
in the Adelaide Evening Journal is soliciting
contributions for a Christmas-dinner for the waifs
of London, and at the end of last month he had
collected .£3O for that object. Meanwhile, so far
as he is concerned, the waifs of Australia may
look for their Xmas-dinner in the rubbish-box at
the back of the sixpenny restaurant, as usual.

TO PLEASURE, HOLIDAY, AND HEALTH RESORTS.
The Commissioners have arranged to give special facilities for visiting the various districts of the Colony
Summer Season, and invite those in search ot Health and Recreation to consult the posters exhibited pn stations,
tables, which contain the latest information regarding trains, fares, and inducements offered to patronise the Railway
during the ensuing
and the book time-
's.
Mountainous Districts
EXCURSION TICKETS are issued at Melbourne on FRIDAYS and SATUR-
DAYS to the Mountainous Districts, at extremely reasonable fares, available for
return till the following Monday. In the case of long distance places, such as Beech-
worth, Bright, Toongabbie and Briagolong, the tickets are available till the Saturday
of the following week.
Seaside and Gippsland Lakes.
EXCURSION TICKETS are issued at the principal stations in the Colony at
special rates to the many popular seaside resorts, available for one month, a special
feature being the issue of “ Head of Family ” tickets to enable the holder to travel to
and fro during the month.
SPECIAL INDUCEMENTS are also offered for visiting SUBURBAN WATER-
ING PLACES at extremely low fares. <
Saturday Half-Holiday Trips
Are arranged at short intervals at very low fares to Fern Tree Gully, Lilydale, Heales-
ville, Yan Yean, Whittlesea, Maceclon, Woodend, Daylesford ; and on suitable days
from Ballarat to Leartnonth, Lai Lai Falls, &c.
At stated intervals Cheap Excursions are arranged from Melbourne to Adelaide and
Sydney, and vice versa, at the following fares :
Melbourne & Adelaide, Ist Class, £2 ; 2nd Class, £1 ss.
Melbourne & Sydney, Ist class, £3; 2nd class, £2.
AVAILABLE FOR ONE MONTH.
Information regarding these latter Excursions, and also full particulars of Intercolonial
train services can be obtained from the Secretaries and Traffic Managers of Railways at
Melbourne, Adelaide and Sydney.
Central Enquiry Office.
For the convenience of Tourists an Enquiry Office has been established at Flinders-street, and intending passengers are invited to call and see Mr. Boyce, the officer in charge,
who will furnish them with information of the latest character relating to trains, and steamer and coach connections, and also supply any railway tickets required.
By Order, R,. Gr. Kent, Secretary for Railways.

The Penang Sugar Estate is situated in the district
of Haki Ilaki, north of the Island of Vitl-Levu, Fiji.
Raid Raki lies in a region termed the “ Dry Belt,”
which latter is now recognised as the best location in
Fiji for sugar-growing—the property is well watered
and irrigated. This estate is peculiarly free from damage
from “ blows.”
The plantation is worked by a staff of nine white men
(including the manager) and about 200 laborers (Indians,
Polynesians, and Fijians).
The estate will give with average seasons an annual
output of 850 to 900 tons at an expenditure of about
£BOOO.
The Property is in excellent order and easily worked,
and the labor well housed, £lOOO having been lately
spent on new coolie houses. The plant is a double crush-
ing one.
Average colored labor employed—
Indian coolies 166
Natives (Fijians) 52
Polynesian 1
Total 219
Tender-forms and all information and particulars
with regard to expenses and output may be obtained from
the undersigned.
No Tender will be entertained unless made on our Form of Tender, and accompanied by
bank draft or marked cheque equal to 10 per cent, of the amount of tender.
Danby, Butler, and FlscUer,
Public Accountants, 66 Elizabeth Street, MELBOURNE.

A Yarrawonga (Vic.) man haa produced a
beautiful flower from a piece of marshmallow,
inoculated with the sap of a pelargonium. Also,
the cohabitation of a milk-thistle with a poppy
has resulted in a particularly novel bloom.
A Mission to Africa.
On another page is given a cartoon of the latest
dodge resorted to by explorers for doing business
with' the poor, unregenerated African heathen.
On account of the terrible massacres in the South
it has been a difficult matter to come into contact
with the principal ivory-selling tribe, “ the
Waziris,” but a thoroughly disinterested philan-
thropist hit upon the idea of doing business and,
at the same time, improving the sad existence of
the well-known “Waziris” by importing a large
supply of Frossard Cavour Cigars, which are the
favourite smoke in Europe in packets of 8 for Is.
First lie distributed a few packets amongst them ;
then, when they clamored for more, as the medium
of exchange was ivory, he erected an enormous
cardboard head on the penny-in-the-slot principle,
so that for every tusk of ivory put in at the top a
packet of Frossard’s Cavour Cigars was thrown
out at the bottom. The rush was immense; the
stock of cigars ran out before the supply of tusks,
so that he was compelled to wend his way to the
coast to ship his cargo and order fresh consign-
ments. The exchange was satisfactory to both
parties, and goes to show how popular these fra-
grant smokes are with everybody. In fact, we
cannot give any better advice to our readers,
whether white or black, than to try these weeds
after their Xmas banquet, and they will find that
although Frossard’s Oavours, like many other
things, end in smoke, “ All’s well that ends
well.”*
The lank, flat-footed agriculturist with the
black-burnt face, who drives the cows home, is
hereby admitted to be a tap-root of this great
country’s prosperity. He doesn’t talk much, and
he invariably dips his dirty hand in the milk-
bucket to get a good grip of the teat, but you can
see he’s thinking hard all the time. What he’s
after is an improved kind of cow with three
udders—one producing milk, the second best fac-
tory butter, and the third prime old Stilton cheese.
When he can invent that cow he knows his for-
tune’s made, and this is what he ponders in the
depth of that stupendous silence. In the mean-
time, all of him who are provident, and enter-
prising, and know what’s what, have purchased
one of those patent staircases to a farmer’s heaven
—the Little Giant Cream Machine Separator and
the Safety Hand Separator. These are the
simplest, cheapest, and most effective separators
known. Just write to the sole agents for N.S.W.,
Denham Bros., 154 Sussex-street, Sydney.*

Two wrongs do not make a right: do
three? Ist Squatter on the Speewa : “That
Secundus is a scoundrel. He’s killing my cattle
for rations, and I’rn bound to keep pace with him
by using his sheep.” 2nd Squatter: “Primus
thinks he has me when he makes his dummies and
cooks kill my sheep. One beef of his is worth 20
monkeys.” Next we have the opinion of Tertius,
a German selector, on both of them : “ Yell, I
don’t know vat makes men zuch roaks, von to de
oder. Brimus and Segundus both shteals their
meat from each oder, und dey subly me mid vree
beef und mutton, zo I shall hold mein tongue.”
Nervous sufferers will find ifc worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advb. on page
33 of this issue.
A reporter on a paper recently established
in the North by an Oriental gentleman, had the
temerity to put in a bill for 2s. for a fortnight’s
“expenses.” The proprietor looked at it and
heaved a bitter sigh. “ This is simply ruinous,”
said he; “I started this paper with the idea of
working it cheap, but here I get extras that are
just crushing. 1 shall have to think over it.”
“But,” said the humble scribe, fingering his hat
nervously, “ the 2s. is for expenses out of pocket.”
“Out of my pocket, young man,” said the pro-
prietor. He is still thinking over it.

I am, yours truly, EDWARD DREW.
A Lady’s Wish Fulfilled.
It gives me great pleasure indeed to inform you that your cele-
brated Alfaline Universal Pills cured me, when all other remedies and
doctors failed, of a stubborn case of Debility ot 18 years’ standing. My
wish is fulfilled at last, and I don’t know how to thank you enough.
Dear Sir, I shall recommend your Alfaline Remedies to all sufferers I
come across. You are quite at liberty to publish or make what use you
like of this testimonial, and anyone writing to me will have full
particulars. I am, yours respectfully, Mrs. E. SMITH, Port Mac-
quarie, Hastings River, N.S.W.
A Miraculous Cure.
The father of Mr. Peter Larsen, the celebrated Bandmaster to the Queensland
Military, writes as follows:
Ithaca-st., Normanby Hill, Brisbane, Sept. 20, 1892.
To Herr Rassmussen,
Dear Sir,—l am jubilant to inform you that your wonderful
Alfaline Pills completely cured me of a very obstinate internal com-
plaint ; and that, although I am now over 70 years of age, I feel as
strong as any young man, and shall always be grateful to you.
Yours very truly, N. LARSEN.
Severe Nervousness Cured.
Stamp Office, G.P.0., Sydney, Sept. 1, 1892.
To Herb Rassmussen.
Dear Sir, —I am pleased to inform you that your Alfaline Vitality
Pills completely cured me of a serious case of nervousness of 20 years'
standing.—Yours gratefully, H. TOST.
Blood Disease Cured.
Parkes Range, Southern Cross, Western Australia.
I have much pleasure to inform you that your Alfaline Lock Pills
completely cured me of a serious blood affection of three years’ stand-
ing, I shall always be glad to recommend your Alfaline Remedies to
my friends. You are at liberty to publish this testimonial for’the
benefit of other similar sufferers.
Yours gratefully, JAMES NELSON WILLIAMS.
The Liver Cured.
To Herr Rassmussen. 64 Washington-street, Sydney.
Dear Sir, —Your Remedies cured me of a very obstinate and com-
plicated complaint after all the doctors gave me up. I suffered greatly
from the Liver and Nerves. Yours truly, ROBERT ALBURY.

Rassmussen’s Head Depot.

Snalse-Blte Antidote — The New Ctn-e,
Hypodermic Injection of Chlorinated Solution of Lime.
THIE above remedy for the cure of Snake-Bite should be in the possession of every one living in the country. Pro-
fessor Halford, of the University of Melbourne, in a work which he has just published on the Action of Snake
Venom on the Blood, mentions that he has received a contribution from the Pasteur Institute of France, on the
Hypodermic injection of Chlorinated Solution of Lime, as an Antidote to the Venom of Snake-Bite, ; and gives a full
account of a number of test experiments which have been made by Dr. A. Calmette, the discoverer of the Antidote.
These tests have been recently confirmed on a human being by Dr. M'Kenzie, of Korrumburra, Vic.
The directions how to use the Antidote are simple, and can be readily understood by anyone of ordinary intel-
ligence. If the services of a doctor cannot be obtained within half-an-hour after the person has been bitten, the
Antidote should be immediately injected according to the directions which are printed on the bottle containing the
solution.
The Concentrated Solution is specially prepared by
